HISTORICAL DEATH-BEDS.
A pleasant essay (if one had time—and
science, in whose pursuit we spend our
time, unfairly robs us of the hope of com-
pensating eternity) might be made out of
historical death-beds—not the beds them-
selves, but the way historians have treated
them. For example, take the death of
Philip II. of Spain, from a recent article in
Temple Bar :—
And so little by little life flickered, and then
faded out, and silently Philip If. passed into the
land of shadows at 5 a.m., when the da* u was
just breaking, and w T hile the choir-boys or the
seminary were chanting the Mass of Nations He
died on September 13, 1598, on the same day that,
14 years before, the last stone had been laid to the
Eseo'-ial. Philip II. departed this life , aged 72,
hav ing miserably misgovei ned Spain for 43 years.
Here’s a rhetorician who is artful (witness his
allusions to thd ehoir-buys and the Escorial)
but not able—ft is decidedly awkward to
kill poor Philip thrice in three ways in three
sentences. And the “5 a.m.” lacks dignity,
and the last sentence is horribly tagged on,
and even the sounding “miserably mis-
governed ” might be cavilled at.
Compare,this with another recent death-
bed—Mary, Queen of Scots’ (how inevitably
those four words seem to rattle off), in Mrs.
Maxwell Scott’s “ Tragedy of Fotheringay ’:
The queen, who was seated on her stool, un-
bound, and still bolding her crucifix, raised bar
head and stretched out her,neck, thinking she was
to be beheaded with a sword, according to the
privilege granted in France to royal pe sons.
“ vly God, ' she said fervent ly, “ I have hoped in
Thee; t give back my soul into Thy hands.”
■ The executioners, seeing her mistake, assisted
her to rise, and conducted bar to the
block, where they made he.- kneel down,
and as she knelt upright, still thinking
she was to be beheaded with the sword, they
made her lie fiat, with her head on the low block,,
o.i yaiew inches high. .As the queen repeated
the Words “ ) irte Domine speravi,” Lord Shrews-
bury raised his wand to give the fatal signal. The
e ecutionoi* lifted the axe, but stopped at a sign
fr i.u his assstant, who had perceived that the
q icen, to enable herself to breathe, had placed her
hands unde her chin. Mary continued to pray
aloud, and in the deep silence that reigned in the
hall she could be heard repeating the verse, “In
mantis tuas Domine conunendo.’ These were her
last w/ds. The executioner, affected, perhaps,
by sympathy and by the general emotion visible
among the bystanders, struck with an ill-assured
aim and only wounded the queen severely, but
she neither moved nor made a sound. At the
blow the soul of Mary Stuart passed to its eternal
reward. 0
This is a plain, straightforward picture,
not particularly impressive. “Reward”
simply means that Mrs. Scott has succumbed
(unlike Fronde) to the glamour of the most
fascinating woman in history, and that her
mat d ials have been chiefly drawn from the
diary of Dominique Bourgoing", Mary’s last
physician.
And so one might quote Motley’s death of
Egiuout : and Macaulay’s execution of Mon-
mouth (with the splendid reference to West-
minster Abbey, and the cunning clinching
sentence, “ Such was the dust with which
the dust of Monmouth mingled”); and
many similar descriptions, with considerable
literary interest and profit in the comparison.

SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Longfellow’s “complete poetical works”
are given in the new Cambridge edition (4s.
3d.), which can be recommended as the best
issued at the price. The text is a reprint of
that of the Riverside edition, and only the
notes are omitted. Of these, however, a
very full appendix collects the most valuable.
A “Life of Nelson” in the “English
Men of Action ’’ series (3s. lOd.) is a com-
pact and capable little biography.
Airs. Cafiyn’s new book, “ A Comedy of
Spasms” (Is. 9d.), is decidedly her best
work so far - , though it makes her limita-
tions still clearer. But it is more
natural than “A Yellow Aster,” more
interesting than “Children of Circum-
stance,” and (crowning merit !) much
shorter than either . The sex-problems
round which the story gravitates do
indeed begin to grow tiresome, and there is
no novelty in the treatment and no strength
in the climax But the book is readable,
and there are here and there observations
and criticisms which have merit enough to
be worth remembering for a week or two.
The heroine is an Australian girl, and “ she
runs amuck in the husband line—duesn t
know what she wants until it’s too late ’—and
then settles down to endurance mainly be-
cause the terlinm quid is too honorable or too
foolish-to encourage her to revolt.
“ Anyway, she’ll never, in any circumstances,
look defeated.”
“ Defeated ! ”
“ Haven’t > ou noticed the look on scores of the
faces of quite fairly-intelligent, God-fearing Eng-
lish girls ? It makes me sick. Yet it’s disgust-
ingly pathetic. They have never had the chance
to become of significance, with all the capacity for
significance lying dormant in them and crying
aloud for satisfaction and an audience. There s
one thing worse, however, that tragic, striving-to-
please air; it attacks ’em at about thirty-nine ;
you see it in that perennial hungry smile, and a
patient attempt to hide the gold in their teeth fil-
lings. I am always inclined to beg the woman to
go to her room, take off her smile, and rest in a
nice padded dressing-gown with her toes on the
fender. A proper appreciation of comfort is the
basis of a sound morality ; it rounds the angles of
your neighbors, and is better than charity as a
cloak for their sins.”
This gives a fair glimpse of the conversa-
tion in “ A Comedy of Spasms.”
NOTES.
“ It is the poets who save the language
from decay, and who keep high the standard
of literary excellence,” writes Edmund
Gosse in the North American Review.
Now we know in what category to place the
anomalous bush bard. He’s a literary anti-
septic.
“Of all the romances that have come to
hand since New Year’s Day the present
reviewer ” —a Saturday Review writer—
“failing a Stanley Weyman, would not
trouble himself to walk three yards to rescue
any from destruction, save only * Almayer’s
Folly,’ ‘ The Martyred Fool,’ ‘ A Business in
Great Waters,’ and ‘ When Valmond Came
to Pontiac. ’” And none of those takes
higher than second-class rank. Literature
nowadays is a hurry-scurry for dollars, and a
“popular” author cannot give quality as
well as quantity.

To Correspondent.— B. Ray ; The Twain-
Hartc rivalry is only a less important repetition
of that between Dickens and Thackeray—emotion
v. intellect. The emotional writer commands a
huge audiencp than the intellectual, yet, even in
the midst of plaudits, he has an uneasy feeling
that his vi ork stands on a lower literary plane.
Bret Harte’s later works harp rather feebly on
monotonous strings, „ but his early stuff has a
power and brilliance that Mark Twain, with all
his merit, never rivalled. Humorous compression,
too, has more of the quality of permanence than
humorous expansion.

Medical.
IT MAY BE SELDOM
That Medical Advertisements can be relied on, but
we here assert the following facts, which can be
borne out from Testimonials in our possession.
Thousands may see this, and treat it as some
announcements deserve, but in this they would

A DISEASE, not a CRIME.
The constant abuse of alcohol fosters a crave
a crave insatiable and deadly. It deprives valu-
able men of all reason and respect; it drives them
to acts of indecency and crime, and to drink what
their palates may even repel. At this stage
pledges and punishment prove useless, and
physical, as well as mental attention, is urgently
required.
The Orient Institute
has established a wonderful reputation in the
curing of this awful disease, and, during the past
18 months, many prominent men of our colony
have been restored to health, sobriety and
position. Alcoholic stimulants become not only
unnecessary, but, in the majority of cases, un-
palatable. Patients undergo no inconvenience or
restraint, and are free to eat and drink what they
like.

work, and was thereby induced to send my friend to you.

appear to be thoroughly cured of drinking, but his
appearance, which had been degenerating, has improved,
and his color is healthy and bright. In short, the man

The old doggerel—

BELLS ON HER TOES,
SHE SHALL HAVE MUSIO
WHEREVER SHE GOBS,
might well be used, substituting LACE for
RINGS, and something about FAIRY-LIKE
FLOSSINESS for the musical interlude. It is
A Lace Season.
There is always a demand for LACE; there
has been since L ACE was first made, and there
will be as long as L ACE is. And LACE is a very
different thing from what it used to be. The
equivalent of LACE that used to cost a big round
sum may now be had for a few shillings, and
ladies “ so dispoged,” as Mrs. Gamp puts it, may
cover themselves and their families with LACE
for less than a cuff or a collar used to cost.
AN OLD WRITER of the Sixteenth Century,
no less a person than LORD BACON, says
“Our English dames are much given to the wear-
ing of costly laces ; and if they be brought from
Italy they are in great esteem.’'
FASHION, LIKE HISTORY, repeats itself J
and Australian ladies, following the fashion, wear
beautiful Laces. No trip to Italy, or to any other
country, is, however, necessary. OUR FAIR

Various Verses .
INVICTA SPES.
(A reply to “Wyvis,” BULLETIN, 10/8/1895.)
To drivel no religious consolation
Take I the pen ; no gods or creeds I own,
No ghostly faitli supplies my inspiration,
No rnetaphysic lore of things unknown.
This much I know : I live 1 and all things being
Join for my life, live in my thought, my act;
One dust-mote strikes the sea—the ripples fleeing
Speed to Infinity : who notes this fact
Knows that all things, all thought, all time, all
space,
Live in him now, and he in them doth blend
Each moment. Lo ! his universal race
Changes in form and sense, but has no end !
Think not I speak of atoms only. Life
Is force that works—not the mere tool of clay.
All works to all. The Universe is rife
With vital impresses, new-born each day,
Creating, as created. I must die
In form and sense, but form and sense live yet,
Shaped from my past. Enough it is that I
Do well, to better these. Ido forget
The babe that once I was ; it knew not me ;
Dead are its flesh, its form, its sense; yet say,
Was it in vain the vanished babe should be,
Shaping the man who lives and joys to-day ?
Enough ! So learn, life is a thing of splendor,
And even suffering holds a keener joy ;
The very voice of your despair may render
Wisdom to others for their strength’s employ.
In such, you live, rejoicing. Feeling this,
Who can despair ? I know no fears or pains
But of the moment, and my reasoned bliss
Firm, undismayed, invincible remains
Within the higher thought. Quit gloomy dream-
ing !
Up, play the man ! and with the evil cope!
Read upon Nature’s path the signpost gleaming—
“ Hope lives for all but those who will not
hope! ”
Mel b.
J. A. ANDREWS.
THE MINAHS—SPRING IN STUDLEY
PARK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sweet! sweet! sweet! From the chrome-
crowned wattles, fleet
Rise we when the sun’s a-beam :
Sweet! sweet! sweet! High on the gum we meet
The moon’s first curving gleam.
Good! good! good! are the noon-rays ’thwart
the wood,
And the shadows on the down :
Good ! good ! good ! is the leaf that gives the food,
And the bloom that makes the crown.
Close ! close ! close ! we nestle and we doze,
Thralled behind our leafy bars ;
Close ! close ! close ! while the silver moon arose,
And her sickle reaped the silver stars.
Kiss ! kiss ! kiss ! All time’s the time for this—
Tho’ a leaf-breath make the timid hen to start.
Kiss ! kiss ! kiss ! Other seasons are amiss ;
People in this season never part.
Fine! fine! fine! is the black wood-bloom as wine—
We sip it with a down-hung head ;
Fine ! fine ! fine ! is the tree that bids us dine,
The tree wherein we drink, and make our bed.
Sway ! sway ! sway ! lazy-winged the long noon-
day,
We hang upon the wind-swept bough ;
Sway ! sway ! sway ! Scent of honey and of hay—
And there is no other day but now.
Wed ! wed ! wed ! we’ll make our marriage-bed
Here among the blaokwood’s tumbled bloom!
Wed ! wed ! wed ! and its sips of nectar shed
Shall diamond the wainscots of our room.
Wed ! wed ! wed ! For 0, my love
Couches for the lives that are to come ;
Wed! wed! wed! Pile the pillows for her head —
The head of her who makes the house a home.
Fine ! fine ! fine ! for the world is all a-shine,
And all the joy of nesting just begun.
Fine ! fine ! fine ! are the eggs of yours and mine,
And their beauty lies a-speckling in the sun.
True ! true ! true ! are the eggs of me and you—
True shall all the egg-born be ;
And the time has come to love, and the time has
come to do,
And fine is all the world and free.
“Tweet! tweet! tweet!” from the withered
blossom sweet —
Summer now has vanquished spring.
“ Tweet! tweet! tweet 1 ” O, we bring you first
food, fleet
As the summer airs the summer leafage fling !
“ Tweet! tweet ! tweet ! ” In this nest the
seasons meet —
Spring and summer, autumn—all in one :
“ Tweet! tweet! tweet! ” in the hazy summer
heat
Love and we our task have almost done.
Sway ! sway ! sway ! Through this summer of a
day !
Life was made for work and love and dreams.
True ! true ! true ! may your lover be to you,
Mild the air and bland the noon-day beams !
Good ! good! good ! is the golden world and
wood :
This chrome-crowned spring shall be the only
one !
Fine! fine! fine ! And we drink the world like
wine,
And preen our wings, and laugh full at the sun!
Studley Park, Melb. Randolph Bkufokd.
The new Anti-Chinese League of Welling-
ton intends establishing a central vegetable-
market in Wellington, to which the suburban
farmers will send their stuff, which will be re-
ceived by a recognised official and distributed to
European hawkers, whose rounds will be so
defined as not to clash with one another. If any
scheme is workable enough to cope with cheap
John, this one will; but the difficulty will be in
the white man “coming down” to take a
“ thruppenny order.”

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-out No. 1,” “Cameo’’and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ THREE CASTLES 77 and ££ G-A^ST-A-IDP. 7 '
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Witts’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
- favor of the
“Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Agents for W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Ltd.)

66 Yankee Doodle , 99 Mended with Polities .
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE ” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has ever
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “YANKEE DOODLE” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford to
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brandsCON-
QUEROR,” “ SIR ROGER,” “ CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE'
ranks supreme.

Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of ln that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
*Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible far its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 12, 1895.
The Curse of Labor Legislation.
Investors who have sunk their money in New Zealand
have very little to be thankful for. However, we can
take a lesson which should be a salutary one not to risk
capita) in countries where representatives of labor have
command of the helm The Bank of New Zealand
ought to have been one of the strongest banks in the
colonies, but when you place men in the position of law-
makers whose only idea is to reduce everything to a com-
mon or uniform level, the consequences are not difficult
to foretell.— Thompson’s Stock and Shave Report, Sydney
I was through New Zealand 16 years ago. .... But it
struck me that people are not now as happy as they were
in those days, and that, I think, is owing to the Legisla-
ture of the country .... the best franchise in vogue in
the colonies is that of Tasmania, where a man has no vote
unless he earns a certain mimimum wage per annum.
I have no objection to women who have a stake in the
country being granted the franchise, but I do not agree
with the one-woman-one-vote idea The country
has not fulfilled the great future I anticipated for it 16
years ago. . . . Man alone is trying to ruin it. ... Par-
liament consists of paid members. . . . I have returned
stronger than ever in the belief that an unpaid Parliament
is the most perfect form of n presentative government.—
Frederic Villiers, Anglo-Choiu-Jap war artist.
The two extracts quoted above are interest-
ing as representing the average reasoning
process of the very average human being.
The ordinary Tory—and most Tories are
painfully ordinary—travels on the simple
and beautiful assumption that every disaster
which falls on a country governed upon
Liberal principles is the natural and inevi-
table result of those principles, and every
disaster which befalls a country governed on
strict Tory principles is not the result of
those principles, but arises from wholly un-
avoidable causes. And the narrow, super-
ficial British globe-trotter, whenever he
finds a country which is not governed exactly
after the British pattern, at once concludes
that whatever goes wrong with it is caused
by its deviation from British principles.
This, of course, necessitates the assumption
that nothing ever goes seriously wrong with
a country that is governed on strictly J.
Bull methods—that there is no starvation
in London or other great cities, no want or
hunger in the Scotch Highlands, no steady
depopulation and constant discontent in Ire-
land, no sweating, no pauperism, no sub-
merged tenth, and that Britain is a land of
peace and harmony generally. This is a
large order, but the Villiers type of globe-
trotter is quite equal to it,
Sixteen years ago, as Frederic Villiers
rightly states, Maoriland was a vast deal
happier than it now is. There was no
Labor element in politics in those days, and
the Bank of New Zealand paid 15 per cent,
dividends then, and the Loan and Mercantile
Co. had not struck its awful snag, and the
revenue left a big surplus over expenditure,
and wages were higher, and land kept rising
' in value, and the people had not lost confi-
dence in their financial institutions as they
now have, and squatting and fanning both
paid very much better than they do to-day,
' and the cities were growing fast, and gold-
mining was more profitable, and many other
beautiful things were happening. Maori-
’ land was a bright and joyous land to inhabit
in those days when there was no Liberal
Government, or one-man-one-vote, or one-
■) woman-one-ditto, and the country travelled
I mainly on sound British principles. And
> now, as Villiers truly observes, Maoriland
is ruled by paid politicians, and has a strongly
Democratic Government, and all these
[ beautiful circumstances have departed never,
l apparently, to return ; therefore, Villiers
J assumes that the Democratic Government is
necessarily the reason of their vanishment.
! But, as it happens, the circumstances are the
J other side up. It was the vanishment of all
these past glories which was, and is, the
* reason for the present Democratic Govern-
’ ment. And instead of labor legislation
causing the fall of Maoriland’s big bank, the
, obvious crumbling of that bank was one of
. the things which caused the labor legislation,
and many other things besides.
i * * *
1 The prosperity of Maoriland 16 years
ago was hollow and uureal, as was also that
'■ of the other Australias. It was created by
I borrowing money at the rate of about 2J
millions per annum, equal to about £8 a
year for each inhabitant—which is much the
• same as if Great Britain borrowed abroad
f At the rate of £300,000,000 a year. Also,
! the Treasury then made another £3 a year
' for each inhabitant out of land-sales, and so
. long as the borrowing and the public lands
\ lasted all was well. The superficial Villiers
saw the country in those days, and concluded
that its state of prosperity was healthy and
; natural, probably because there being no
foreign borrowing and no public land-sales
1 in England he could not realise the existence
of such things elsewhere. And then both
these sources of revenue became worn out,
and Maoriland had to send away some £2 7s.
annually per inhabitant for interest on the
, cost of those bye-gone revels. If Great
■ Britain, the country that Villiers comes
’ from, were similarly burdened in proportion
to population, its interest bill would be some-
' thing like £100,000,000 per annum, or more
than its entire present revenue, and all that
amount would leave the country yearly.
The old semi-Tory Governments of the past
built up this stupendous liability, and it was
mainly their utter incapacity to deal with the
monster they had created that forced the so-
called Labor Ministry of to-day into exis-
tence. But the people are certainly not so
1 happy under the new regime as under the
old. The old Tory days were the drunk
over-night ; the present Democratic regime
is the recovery in the morning. And the
drunk which Maoriland had in those by-gone
times, when it was governed under the system
which Villiers so cordially approves, was
such as no other country outside Australasia
ever had without defaulting when the re-
covery set it,
• * *
It is quite possible, as alleged, that
Labor legislation had something to do with
the awful smash of the Bank of New Zealand
anti other institutions. The sudden end
°f the boom made a tremendous depre-
ciation in land-values, and the land which
the big bank had accumulated went down
with the rest. The fall in the prices of pro-
duce in Europe made more depreciation.
The land-tax made still another drop. But
the land-tax was imposed as a dire necessity
by a Government which had to tax every-
thing in sight in order to raise revenue to
meet the liabilities bequeathed to it by its
good old Conservative predecessors. More-
over, the bank’s troubles and the land-tax
both began years before the first Labor
administration took office. And in New
South Wales, Victoria, and Queensland,
where there has never been any Labor
Government, or any land-tax worth men-
tioning, or any woman-suffrage, the same
financial disaster arrived in an even worse
form, and is still with us. Therefore, it
would seem that a strictly Fat Man form of
Government is quite as fatal to capital as the
other kind, if not more so. And in Victoria,
as in Maoriland, the first really Liberal
Government came into existence after the
crash, when the palsied hand of Fat proved
utterly unable to cope with the disasters it
had brought about. The bursting of one
boom made Turner, just as the bursting of
another made Ballance, and Victoria also
was far happier under the Fat Men of
the boom days than it is now, for the same
reason that the young Juggins who is
spending his fortune is happier than the old
reformed Juggins who is sorrowfully looking
back on the fortune that is spent.
• • •
Sixteen or twenty years ago hardly anyone
in Australasia seriously cared about demo-
cratic government, or one-man-one-vote, or
woman suffrage, or checking the accumula-
tion of big estates, or State banks, or any
other of the political theories of to-day. In
those times there was, comparatively speak-
ing, abundance of money—borrowed money
mostly—and an immense revenue was derived
by trading away the public estate, and wool
was nearly twice its present value, and
because of the abundance of money work
was much more plentiful than it now
is, and wages were high, and profits
and dividends were good. A country
which enjoys all these blessings never
worries much about political problems ; the
kind of Government which produces such
results is good enough for any average com-
munity. It was the leanness and famine of
after years that created the present demo-
cratic party in politics ; if the old orgie
could have gone on for ever the Fat Man
might have governed the country indefinitely.
The new Labor element may not have done
much, and it may have experimented wildly
and foolishly, but its circumstances are so
unprecedented that things could hardly be
otherwise. It was created on the ruins
of the wildest financial outbreak in
modern history, with general instructions to
find some method by which a prematurely
aged community could be ground up in the
mills of God and made young again—to dis-
cover some solution for the problem of
poverty and want of work which no one has
ever yet solved. The landlords, finan-
ciers, Fat Men, and all the “respectable”
elements of the community held that
these evils were a part of the natural order
of things, and they refused to attempt a
solution of the problem—in most cases they
refused even to admit that there was any
problem to solve. Therefore, the hungry
masses took to sending printers, miners,
shearers, and pick-and-shovel men into the
Legislature. They also have failed to solve
the question—failed utterly in the vastness
of their ignorance and inexperience ; but, at
least, they went one better than their pre-
decessors, for they made an attempt —an
honest attempt in the main—to put things
right. And, if it be finally written of them
that they tried and failed, it will be at least
a nobler epitaph than that of their Fat Man
predecessors, who never tried at all.
» # »
South Australia and Maoriland, the most
distinctly Democratic of the seven provinces,
are just now suffering—the latter especially—
from a violent attack of experimental legisla-
tion. Many of the experiments will probably
prove futile, and dozens of the measures now
being passed will be repealed in disgust a few
years hence. These more or less blind
efforts to find a remedy for the social evil
which is as old as original sin are doubtless
very ridiculous—everything is more or less
ridiculous. England, until within the last
few years, avoided the question by simple
and truly British devices. At one time it
made vagrancy a capital offence, and kept
down the great problem of poverty by hang-
ing the very poor. Then it took to en-
couraging emigration, and for very many
years shipped off its great social difficulty in
huge masses to Canada, the United States,
South Africa, Australia, Maoriland, and
elsewhere, and thus passed on most of the
great unanswerable conundrum to some one
else. It has still profound faith in the advan-
tages of exporting its national conundrum,
as is evidenced by the encouragement which
is lent to Booth’s scheme for dumping down
the worst section of British pauperism on
the doorstep of the “colonies.” Austral-
asia, having no convenient place for export,
has to try to find a remedy on its own
ground, and its efforts in this direction
naturally shock the British globe-trotter,
who believes, because his own country has
“ moved on ” its poor for many years, that
the same moving-on process is the natural
and only legitimate treatment—at all events,
he has nothing better to suggest. Alto-
gether, there is little comfort and less good
advice to be obtained from the Villiers
variety of councillor, and the views of that
superficial person cast no new light on any-
thing.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Prayer for Rain and Prayer for Work.
A few weeks ago the churches offered up
special prayers for rain in New South Wales at
a time when it was badly wanted. The
rain arrived a few hours afterwards, and it
has been arriving more or less ever since. The
inevitable result is that the churches are suffer-
ing very badly from swelled head, and keep
pointing out, for the billionth time or there-
abouts, that the efficacy of prayer has been
fully demonstrated, and that the Church, as an
institution which knows how to pray effec-
tively, is absolutely essential to the welfare of
the community. And the Reid Government
has gone out of its way to cadge for votes on
the ground that it believes in the Church—
somewhat after the fashion of the man who
tries to attain sudden importance on the ground
that he once lived next door to the brother of
a celebrated criminal just hanged.
“ I belong,” said Jacob Garrard, “ to a Ministry which
recognises God over all. The country had been languish-
ing for something that no Ministry could give—that no
political creed could supply, and the Ministry had asked
the people to join in an appeal to God. Those who
laughed and scoffed at the idea of the Ministry setting
apart a day for humiliation and prayer for rain had their
mouths closed by the ready answer to the prayers of the
people.”
All this grows very wearisome, and the contro-
versy about the efficacy of prayer gets no
further advanced. Therefore, The Bulletin
rises to suggest a reasonable, simple, and effec-
tive test. Let the Churches pray that lack of
employment shall wholly cease in Australia,
and that there shall be work and sufficient
wages for all who want work—not sand-shifting
or other useless State relief done with borrowed
money, but useful employment. The clergy
have never seriously tackled this question yet,
though it is even more essential than the rain
difficulty. Want of rain is only a temporary
trouble; want of work, and consequent want
of food, have burdened mankind for count-
less ages. Moreover, rain always comes,
sooner or later, whether the Churches pray
or not, therefore they know that rain
will follow prayer because it follows every-
thing in course of time. It even follows
battles, hangings, sudden deaths, epileptic fits,
disembowelment per circular-saw, prize-fights,
riots, Armenian atrocities, and murders, so the
fact that it arrives after prayer is not surpris-
ing. The church prays unitedly and conspicu-
ously for rain, knowing that it must come in
any case. If it believes in its own creed let it
pray as loudly and unitedly for the other thing.
Abundance of work doesn’t come in any case.
It is much more wanted than rain. And if it
comes it will be a much more convincing
answer. In short, let the churches keep on, if
they like, praying for the thing that comes
without prayer, but let them also pray for the
thing that doesn’t come without prayer. The
perpetual desire to lift the light end of the log
and the constant avoidance of the heavy end
look like a great want of faith on the part of
the local cleric.
“A Social Cancer.”
Editor Bulletin, —Your article (Sept. 21) “A
Social Cancer ” touches upon a very great evil
which demands serious attention. You rightly
deplore dealing with effects instead of causes,
yet you have fallen into that very mistake your-
self. Motherhood should not be a disgrace,
and will not be when women are free; but, at
present, motherhood is unhappily disgraced,
and as much inside marriage as out of it. You
describe motherhood as “ the supreme self-
sacrifice ; ” surely that description is illogical,
unless a woman willingly takes upon herself the
functions of motherhood. Forced self-sacrifice
is a contradiction in terms, and all motherhood
that is not willing is disgraced, no matter what
priest or clergyman may have declared it legal.
You blame women for the present state of
affairs, yet men alone have had the making of
the laws, and it is they who have branded as
“illegitimate” a child born out of wedlock,
and who have thrown the whole responsibility
on to the unfortunate woman. It is men who
have decreed that a child born in wedlock
belongs solely to the father, the married mother
having no rights ; and it is they who, by their
unequal divorce laws, have promulgated the
devil’s doctrine that immorality is a crime in
woman, but only a venial offence in man. Since
women have had no voice in making the laws,
why should the whole responsibility of our un-
just social system be thrown on them ? Again,
with regard to conventional religion, is it not
men-priests and ministers—who have done their
best to keep women in subjection, and to pre-
vent them from taking an active part in
national life ? and have not men, as a whole,
striven to keep women in ignorance of the
seamy side of life ? What has been the reward
of those women who have broken the con-
spiracy of silence and have protested against
injustice in every shape and form ? Would it
not be better, instead of blaming women for
their acquiescence (in a vast majority of cases
unconscious acquiescence) in the degradation of
their sisters involved by the false standards set
up by male lawgivers and priests, for men and
women to cease throwing stones at one another,
and to work together to bring in a truer and
higher ideal of life and to make our cities ap-
proximate slightly to the ideal laid down by
Whitman, whose great city is “ where there are
the greatest men and women, and where the
cleanliness of the sexes stands?” Your proposed
remedy for the present state of things would
only increase the evil. According "to your
article, the State is practically to relieve a
father and mother of all duties and responsibili-
ties with regard to their children. Is mother-
hood, then, purely a physical function, and
nothing else? If parentage is such a holy
thing as you say, why are the children deprived
of the mother’s care and of the father’s train-
ing? Or does the State propose to provide
mother-love at so much per pound ? What we
want is not less parental responsibility, but
more ; and the great aim of the "Woman Move-
ment is to secure equal justice between man
and woman, and to uplift the sacred responsi-
bility of parenthood, which has too long been
sacrificed to the insatiable Moloch of Lust.—A
New Woman.
[Tub Bulletin gives this space as a representative reply,
and diffidently adds a few remarks. (I>. Doesn’t a New
Woman willingly become a mother when she marries?
Isn’t her marriage a voluntary contract? Doesn’t she
know what it implies? If she doesn’t, how is society to
teach her? Doesn’t she read the evening newspapers?
Society heavily punishes the male concerned in forced
sexual union ; when once a New Woman says “ I will ” to
voluntary sexual union, and the wicked wolf locks his
front door, how is society to save the innocent lamb?
(2). The Bulletin does not, and did not, wholly
blame women for the present state of affairs.
It doesn’t blame anybody—only Things As They Are. It
has pointed out that the higher the standard of chastity,
the lower the depth of complementary prostitution ; also
that by the dread of her sex’s general scorn and pity for
a “fallen woman,’’ and by her own sense that illegitimate
motherhood was a “disgrace,” the Sydney- victim was
driven to the abortionist. The Bulletin attributed her
death to “Society,” “social traditions”—for which men
are doubtless as responsible as women. The Bulletin cor-
dially denounces those men. It never did otherwise. Why
won’ttheNew Woman weighherwords—andotherpeople’s?
(2). This paper didn’t propose “a remedy for the present
state of things ” in the large and airy fashion of the New
Woman. There is no “ remedy for the present state of
things;” and as “the present state of things” has arrived
by a very slow process of evolution, permanent improve-
ment can only come through more evolution. This paper
did propose a palliative for abortion, and does so still, in
the shape of a State hospital, with no questions asked,
and women (except patients)shoo’d into the next county.
“ Why are the children deprived of the mother’s care and
the father’s training?” Because these articles, left to
themselves, mean suicide, as in Adelaide ; murder, as in
Melbourne ; and abortion, as in Sydney ; and because
State parentage, though objectionable in many ways, is
infinitely preferable to these things.— Ed. B.]
The Law’s Unfixedness.
In a Maoriland town, the other day, a hotel-
keeper was charged with “ allowing women of
ill-fame to remain on his premises longer than
was necessary for reasonable refreshment.”
Very naturally he rose and asked how long the
Bench considered was necessary for a woman
of ill-fame to have reasonable refreshment, and
the Bench was hopelessly stumped by the
question, but it went right on with the case


none the less, and delivered judgment. This
is another example of how the law dodges its
responsibilities. Quite recently an Adelaide
Bench decided, to the surprise of every
one, that betting on commission wasn’t
betting within the meaning of the Act, but,
though the law thus decided that it wasn’t
the thing people supposed it to be, it
declined to give any expression of opinion
as to what kind of thing it was, so that
now no one knows what it is. A Melbourne
Court lately ruled that a man who laid odds on
the pavement couldn’t be prosecuted, because
the pavement wasn’ta “fixed place.” Common-
sense would have decided that it was a fixed
place, and that it was the man who was unfixed,
seeing that he moved about to different parts of
the pavement, but the “ statoot ” regarded it
otherwise. Yet, though it decided that the
pavement wasn’t fixed, it didn’t explain how it
had become unfixed, or how unfixed it
was, or how any place can be otherwise
than fixed, so the world is again left in
doubt. And the Maorilaud law now decides
that a lady of loose character mustn’t remain in
a hotel longer than is necessary for reasonable
refreshment, but it won’t say how long is
necessary, or how much refreshment is reason-
able for a lady of loose character, so the blighted
publican must wander forth to wrestle with the
conundrum all alone. Legal retribution is a
hazy thing which arrives mostly by accident.
Sometimes the door is left open audit blows in,
and at other times it arrives in other ways, but
it is almost always intangible and unaccount-
able.
Tlie N.S.W. Free-Postage Scandal.
From a Parliamentary return it appears that
the cost to N.S.W. Treasury of advertising in
the Sydney dailies the lists of names under the
Electoral Act from 21st to 25th Sept., ’95, was
.£348. No wonder the daily papers never
go straight, when in addition to a free postage
subsidy of (say) from .£30,000 to £40,000 a year
they get little pickings such as the foregoing.
Newspaper free - postage is a gross job,
especially when the leading daily (“ Freetrade ”
it calls itself!) of Sydney has the gall to object
to Free Public Education while putting thou-
sands per annum in its proprietary’s pocket, per
medium of the free-postage grab. Newspapers
are purely commercial speculations, and there is
no more public reason for passing Brown’s
newspaper-merchandise through the post free
than there is for free-passing Smith’s gin-
merchandise. There is no more honest reason
for the State giving the newspapers free special
trains every morning than there would be for
it giving brewers Tooth and Toohey a free
beer-train every morning. If you give one
public newspaper free postage you must give it
to every other public newspaper, and in N.S.
Wales every public newspaper and scores of
papers that are not public newspapers are
carried free. This sweating of the taxpayer is
perpetrated in no other Australian province,
and, so far as The Bulletin knows, in no other
country in the world. The free-postage steal,
conducted under the cry of “ education for the
masses,” but really on behalf of a coterie whose
most influential members, the S. M. Herald pro-
prietary, oxdy the other day, wrote down the
Parliamentary proposal to make the N.S.W.
Public-schools really free, on the ground that
the province could not afford to do so, costs
New South Wales about £70,000 a year !
For, in addition to bona - fide newspapers,
every trade-circular masquerading as a news-
paper is carried free by post. To such a length
is the system carried (and in a desperate time
of public need), that not long ago a trading
M. who was issuing a business-circular w'ent
round, got enough advts. to cover the cost of
issue, registered the print as a newspaper at
a cost of a few shillings, and calmly dumped
into the post-office one hundred thousand copies
of his trade-circular, which were carried free
for him by the miserable taxpayer ! And the
N. members of Parliament are too fright-
ened of the newspapers to “ stoush ” the free-
postage reach-out.
“ Pleasant ” Presbyterianism.
The Presbyteries of Melbourne North and
South have decided to wrestle prayerfully with
sundry modern problems. These holy bodies
would fain know whether “pleasant Sunday
afternoons ” are good for religion, and whether
the use of the magic-lantern for illustrating
sacred subjects can be legitimately adopted by
the disciples of John Knox. An ordinary lost
mortal might easily settle both these points if
the Presbyterian Conscience would, in the first
place, state the real object of religion, and, in
the second place, define “ legitimacy ” as re-
lating to Church work.. Presuming that reli-
gion is simply a means of support for parsons,
and a cloak for bank-directors, worldly wisdom
feels bound to warn it against countenancing
any sort of Sunday addresses which don’t
bring grist to the mill. Pew-rents and
sixpences in the plate are the life-blood of pro-
fessional religion. A congregation supports
the parson in order to save its own soul,
and the fraudulent gilded boomster pays
top price for his individual salvation.
Let him and the other people get into the
habit of attending “ pleasant Sunday ” gather-
ings in connection with the Church, where they
can feel pious free of charge—and the financial
character of religion will assuredly suffer. Not
much at the outset; but, as time goes on, and
the afternoon attendance comes to be generally
reckoned as an act of piety, and gets sub-
stituted for attendance at evening service,
religion will lose a bit. No far-seeing Pres-
bytery can favor this new-fangled Sawbath
entertainment, especially if it harbors serious
intentions of working up to its name. A truly
“ pleasant afternoon ” sandwiched between a
theological morning and evening would sooner
or later be singled out for enjoyment, like a
savory slice of ham, whilst the two thick slices
of stale bread were left upon the counter un-
tasted. The Bulletin advises every Presbytery
to place the brand of sin upon the brow of
this P.S.A. idea, and stigmatise it as a devilish
suggestion. With magic-lantern slides for
illustrating sacred subjects the church can
afford to be in cordial sympathy, if the object
of religion is to tempt young people into the
tabernacle. Tun Bulletin recommends the
experiment of a darkened evening-service, a
largo sheet stretched in front of the altar, and
a skilful operator casting suitable views
thereon as the shepherd drones out his
sermon. A magic - lantern is the natural ally
of church religion, because its ways are
dark, and therefore attractive to the young.
Sermons illustrated with views would lure
many an engaged couple from moonlight walks,
although a gloom-enshrouded pew might not
seem preferable to a ramble in the fog. And if
a very hardshell Presbyterian meenister can’t
persuade his conscience to sanction pictures on
the sheet, he can make his sermons equally
popular by turning out the gas. It isn’t “ more
Light ” that the unco guid require. Light is
fatal to dogma and superstition. True faith
is never so happy as when seated in a dim,
religious corner, hugging a dear delusion un-
observed.
In re Dean.
Leak. Bulletin, —I, among, I am sure, hun-
dreds of others, cannot help feeling emphatically
disgusted concerning the attitude of nearly
everybody connected with the Dean case, in-
cluding counsel, defence committee, and daily
press. For instance, it is gravely recorded
that Salomons, Q. 0., believes Dean to be
guilty ; Pilcher, Q.C., believes him to be inno-
cent ; the President of the Royal Commission
was of opinion that Dean’s guilt was not open
to reasonable doubt ; Mr. Goddard, Mr.
Moodie, and other members of the defence
committee, believe him to be innocent; Mr.
Meagher says he is innocent; Mr. Crick
vouches for his innocence ; Mrs. Dean believes
her husband to be guilty ; so do his mother-in-
law and the Attorney-General, and the majority
of the bar—sore at the granting by Government
of a re-opening of the trial; Dean, the party
most interested, believes himself to be inno-
cent; while the Judge who tried him for his
life is as certain that he attempted to poison
his wife as though he had seen him with his
own eyes administering the lethal agencies.
Everybody, in fact, considers it necessary to
deliver a pronouncement concerning Dean’s
innocence or guilt, whereas the only question
involved—a question studiously ignored by the
interested, and absolutely lost sight of by the
unthinking—is to be found in the verdict
of two-thirds of the Royal Commission ap-
pointed to examine and report upon the case,
viz., that the evidence was not sufficient to
substantiate a conviction; in other words,
Dean was released by virtue of a find-
ing by the Commission equivalent to
the Scotch verdict “ Not proven.” It
does not matter a rap whether Dean is
innocent of the crime with which he was
charged, or not. Mr. Pilcher, should not put
himself out in the matter at all. Mr. Goddard,
Mr. Moodie, and the other members of the
committee, are not, and should not be, alarmed
by any subsequent development of the great
Dean case. They did their duty in agitating
for the appointment of the Commission to re-
open the matter. It is of no consequence
whether they knew Dean to be guilty or not;
as far as the safety of the community is con-
cerned, it is of no consequence even whether his
solicitor himself knew Dean to be guilty. The
public interest lies not in Dean’s guilt or in
Dean’s innocence, but in this, viz., that (1) the
evidence submitted to the jury was not such
as to justify a conviction ; and (2), the
jury was obviously and unduly influenced
in its finding by a prejudicial deliverance
from the Judge, already possessing a bias
against the assumption of the prisoner’s inno-
cence. If Dean were a Deeming, and a Peace,
and a Wainewright rolled into one ; and if
the most stainless public characters, including
Aristides himself, believed him guilty ; still,
if there were “ a reasonable doubt ” (which
is a euphemism for “insufficient evidence”)
about his guilt; then, according to the tra-
ditions of that justice, which it is the conser-
vative fashion to denominate as British, but
which is no less Australian or human generally,
there could not possibly be a just conviction. —
Judex.
N.S.W. Mining-Company Law.
Mr. Humphery has introduced in N.S.W.
Leg. Council a Bill to amend the No Liability
Mining Go’s Act, and the Government repre-
sentative has announced Ministerial approval of
it. The Act badly needs amending ; for it is
vague in a dozen places, and, on account of the
absence of schedules, the registration of com-
panies under it is both uncertain and expensive.
Worse still, in view of the Westralian excite-
ment, it is impossible to register under it any
co. which has been formed to work a mine out-
side N.S.W. Mr. Humphery’s Bill seeks to
meet some of the shortcomings which a dozen
years’ experience has exhibited. Two vital
changes are proposed —to permit registration
of a co, to work a mine anywhere; and
to allow the directors to reinstate shares which
have been forfeited for non-payment of calls,
provided the holder pays up after the forfeiture,
which, as now, is to take place without any
resolution on the directors’ part. It is still
proposed that only 10 per cent, of the contri-
buting capital need be paid-up in cash prior to
registration. Ten per cent, is not enough, and
the provision is otherwise bad. The leading
feature of the measure—the feature which gives
to it its name—is that the shareholder may
cease paying calls whenever he pleases. The
No Liability co’s shares are therefore compara-
tively safe things to gamble with. Popular
on this account, professional promoters refuse
to submit any other kind of co. to the public.
Then, with a bare 10 per cent, of the contrihuthuj
capital paid up, and 8 per cent, of that pro-
bably swamped in preliminary expenses and
vendors’ payments, the co., with a nominal
capital of perhaps <£loo,ooo, sets out on its
alleged mining career with a credit balance of
£3OO or ,£4OO. Frequently a call has to be
made within a week after work is really started.
Then begins the issue of those ridiculous official
reports which The Bulletin has often quoted
—desperate reports that are nevertheless neces-
sary in order to draw out the calls. Meantime
a few thousand shares have been judiciously
“placed” and a “rig” begins; the profes-
sional promoter clears out, and, the boom
having eased off, calls refuse to come in, and
another “ mine” is deserted, and the company
glides peacefully into liquidation. The Bul-
LETXN knows Barrier “ mines,” formerly owned
by no - liability mining co’s with nominal
capitals of from ,£50,000 to £lOO,OOO, on which
less than a thousand pounds was spent from
first to last, the work being stopped before it
had got to the point where it could prove any-
thing. The whole secret was that the pro-
moters had got out of their shares, the market
(being flooded with promoters’ shares) refused
to look at the contributing ones, calls remained
unpaid (all prospect of a successful gamble
being over), and the mines remained as much
unproved as ever they were. The worst feature
often was that the fool-prospector undertook
when handing over his leases not to part with
any of his shares for, say, six months. If it is
intended to make so-called mining a mere pre-
text for gambling, well and good ; 10 per cent,
paid up is enough. In fact, the existence of
any mine at all is superfluous; and it should be
sufficient if enough were paid up to pay the
printer for turning out the scrip—only in that
case the promoter and the professional director
would grumble. But, if it is intended to make
companies really serve the mining industry in
some little degree, then we must declare that
before registration is granted 10 or 20 or 30 per
cent, of the contributing capital shall be
actually available for prospecting or mining
purposes. By the way, too, the proposed pro-
vision for the reinstatement of forfeited shares
opens up a cheering prospect before the pro-
fessional new-company director.
The N.S. W. Marine Board gave a somewhat
unaccountable decision when it suspended for
six months the certificate of second officer
Lanfear, of the wrecked Catterthun. Lanfear
took charge of the vessel shortly before the
disaster, and was in charge when the smash
took place. The course had been set by the
captain, and he followed it closely so long as it
seemed safe to do so. When the Great Seal
Rock appeared ahead he promptly altered the
course so as to avoid this unexpected danger. If
he had waited to call the captain before making
the change, and the vessel had struck during I lie
few seconds or minutes thus lost, he would
assuredly have been severely dealt with for his
cowardice in not taking instant action at such
a crisis, on his own responsibility. As the
Catterthun struck anyhow, he is dealt with
because he did act on his own responsi-
bility. The Marine Board suspends Lanfear
for doing what he did, but it hasn’t the moral
courage to say exactly, for the guidance of the
next man so situated, what he should have done.
Therefore, the next man will probably do some-
thing else, and perhaps thereby lose his ship
also, and then the same Board will suspend his
certificate for not doing what Lanfear was
suspended for doing. If the Marine Board
knows, as it professes to do, that Lanfear did
a wrong thing, then it must know what was
the right thing to do. And a Board that knows
the right thing to do, and yet fails to definitely
explain it so as to avert similar catastrophes in
future, is playing it very low down on the com-
munity.
Melbourne dailies have been significantly
silent over the sequel to the sentence of 20
strokes with the birch recently passed by
Justice A’Beckett, with official-medical con-
currence, on a youth found guilty of an obscene
offence. The mental weakness of the miserable
wretch was made so apparent by the circum-
stances of the case and the behaviour of the
offender, that even the jury of laymen expressed
its opinion that he was demented. Official
medical opinion was that the lad was “silly,
weak-willed, and paralysed,” but legally sane—
i.e., he knew right from wrong. Asked by the
Judge if it thought a birching would do the
boy good, the medical department replied that
it thought it would. So the birching was
ordered, together with three months gaol. But
the newly-formed Criminology Society happily
got on the track of the proceeding, and, by suc-
ceeding in the vigorous protest it caused to be
made in Parliament, at once justified its exist-
ence. Very quietly the birching was remitted,
not a word being uttered by the respectable
press upon the subject, before or after.
While Primate Smith was calling upon Premier
Reid in Sydney to proclaim a Day of Thanks-
giving for the rain, a carrier at Broken Hill was
telling his experiences—the most serious of them
occurring on the very days on which the rain
was falling on the roof of the Primate’s palace.
This teamster left Broken Hill with eight good
horses, drawing the small load of five tons of
flour, bran and chaff for Wilcannia. He was
11 days on the track (ordinary time, four to five
days) ; during that period his horses ate tons
of the loading. Receipts for the other tons,
at £5 per ton, £l2 10s. He loaded wool back,
trying the team with only tons. But as the
horses couldn’t eat Wool, and as there was little
else for them, three of the animals died. Then
he threw off the wool by the track, and crept
back with his five horses, just managing to drag
them into town. The trip pans out thus :
Cr.: £l2 10s. Dr.: Driver, five weeks, £lO ;
three horses, £45; feed and water en route, £l5;
sundries (in loading, etc.), £s—total, £75. Loss,
£62 10s. And now he has to get the wool in some-
how. Primate Smith has only got half through
his job. To thanksgive yet is absurdly prema-
ture. Between Jan. and Sept. 1, Sydney had 22in.
of rain. Menindie had less than 4in. ; Bourke,
4|in. ; Wilcannia, 6|in. ; Broken Hill, sin.
Some of these places have, since the “ drought
broke up ” (either in answer to Primate Smith,
or to the supplications addressed to the
new copper Joss lately bought by the
Chinese of Warren for £4 10s.), had as
much as half-an-inch —some not that. None
of them in the Par West have had enough to do
the slightest good to the country. So that just
where it was most wanted the rain has not
fallen —which surely, in the circumstances,
cannot be regarded as that kind of an arrange-
ment of things which the State should take a
hand in thanksgiving for.
F.ditor Bu’-oETIN, The establishment of
lying-in hospitals, in which no questions are
asked and the children born are immediately
taken care of by the State, would undoubtedly
tend to lessen suicide, abortion, and infanti-
cide, though the latter saving would be some-
what counterbalanced by the mortality aqaong
infants consequent on artificial feeding. If the
State undertook to rear all children born in the
land there would be fairness in the establish-
ment of such hospitals ; but if only children
born out of wedlock are received, the man who
marries the woman he loves and does not shirk
the responsibilities of fatherhood, would have,
justly, to support his own progeny, and,
unjustly, to pay taxes to make provision for
the offspring of the bachelor, who, while prac-
tising unlimited polygamy, would have his
possibly enormous family brought up by the
State. Likewise, if the State arranged to
receive all women about to give birth to chil-
dren, there would be no injustice, but if only
unmarried women were received, then the
wedded woman, ready to fulfil her whole duty
to her babe, would still be forced by poverty
to trust herself at the critical time to unskilled
assistance and struggle through if she escaped
with her life, while the'unmarried woman would
receive medical assistance and full scientific
amelioration of her trial. Marriage would thus
become disadvantageous to both sexes, and the
establishment of homes and the institution of the
family would gradually decline among the poorer
classes. Surely it is right that a man should
support his own children, as much so for the
bachelor as for the benedict! It is the unfair
exoneration of the man from his responsibility,
leading as it does to the woman’s frantic
attempt to free herself also, which is the
cause of many of the present social evils.
Payment of sums of money will not cover a
father’s responsibility. All children should
have the full rights of legitimacy, and every
mother the privileges of a legal wife. Many,
women absolutely refuse money from a man for
the support of a child that is not legally con-
sidered his own, feeling that by its acceptance
they would be placed in the position of harlots
—paid after, if not before the , ;rth of the
child.— Wabra.
The new Victorian Governor’s reception, which
was to be quite a modest farce, promises to
grow into a shocking grovel. The idea of send-
ing merely a few M’sL. A. along in the Lady
Loch to meet Brassey’s yacht is now ridiculed
as something much too small for such a gigantic
occasion. The Lady Loch, indeed ! One of
the China Navigation Co.’s steamers will pro-
bably be loaded up with a few hundred thirsty
souls who couldn’t find room aboard the
Government’s private picnic boat. An awful
military and naval display is included in the
programme, and the swearing-in of Brassey at
the Exhibition Building threatens to smash the
record for pomp and circumstance. But it is
at St. Kilda that representative “ loyalty ”
means to do its worst. The Mayor and
Council have struck a scheme for demoralising
unto wormhood the rising generation. Every
schoolmaster in the district is invited, by
circular, to pin a certain reception-badge upon
the coat, cap, or pants of every boy who will
submit to the indignity. The badge is unique
in its puerility, and the rate-payers will have
to pay for the accursed thing. It bears two
inscriptions—“ Welcome Brassey ” and “Ad-
vance Australia ” —calculated to delude children
into a vague belief that the neighbourhood will
get a mighty impetus from Bbassey’s landing
there. One councillor, who keeps a fashionable
hotel, is suspected of an intention to vent his
joy in free beer for the masses on the eventful
day. Other councillors are itching to distin-
guish themselves. St. Kilda is rapidly going
mad, and nothing but a heavy fall of rain on
the Brassey demonstration will save the place
from howling lunacy. Democrats are herewith
invited to pray that the heavens will weep
copiously when Brassey comes ashore.
V illiers, the rampant war correspondent, is of
opinion that Britain should have gone to war
with France rather than allow that country
to occupy Madagascar, which, he holds, is
intended as a base from which to threaten
South Africa and Australia. As a war corres-
pondent, Villiebs finds his bread and butter
in war, and as a Jingo he has a natural yearn-
ing for gore and iron-clads and entrenchments,
and the banging of the big drum, and the up-
heaval of the Br-r-ritish flag. Meanwhile,
events are rapidly proving that he is a very use-
less and misguided variety of Jingo. The
Madagascar question, apparently, will settle
itself, partly through fever-and-ague and forest,
and partly because the bill for these stupendous
military operations is getting altogether too big.
A lot of similar questions would settle themselves
also if the rest of the world would only sit
down and look on. If France wants to be dis-
agreeable to Britain then the fact that it is
fooling away its money and its army in the
swamps of Madagascar makes it all the less able
to create trouble later on. If it ever wants to
attack Australia, which it could do much more
easily from Tonquin or New Caledonia, then
the dead men who have been left in the African
island won’t be available for the purpose at all
events; so there seems every reason why this
country should sit down and look on, and pre-
serve a dead silence. The great art of sitting
down at the right time and keeping quite still
needs to be cultivated a great deal more than
it is.
Louis Becke has now two or three volumes
on the London publishers’ stocks, bub his second
book in order of publication issues from an enter-
prising Sydney source. In “His Native Wife”
there is shown the same mastery of South Sea
Island incident, the same graphic narrative force,
which made “By Reef and Palm” famous.
Becke is a born story-teller, and practice
has given him the story-teller’s knack of
beating his grain of gold into any num-
ber of chapters required. One hurries . over
the preliminaries to get the real thrill of the clos-
ing scenes, in which a cheerful chieftain dives
under water with a missionary and knocks his
head on a jagged rock, while a no less vigorous
chieftainess stabs the missionary’s wife to the
heart, and her sister sails over the horizon witb
her handsome head pillowed on her Portuguese
lover’s bosom. These things are pure Becke, and
excellent reading, and one suffers gladly for their
sake the diluted Becke of the preceding chapters.
“ Kis Native Wife” certainly makes a promising
beginning for Alex. Lindsay’s “ Australander ”
series. Post free from Bulletin office, Is. 4d.

The “Arcadian Eve,” in one act, took
well at Sydney Lyceum, on Saturday, notwith-
standing which it is a thin little affair, which
wouldn’t be visible if it turned sideways. It
relates to a married man, who has a magic ring,
and, when he rubs it in his wife’s absence, a lovely
goddess (Miss Florence Young) bursts in through
the back of the scenery, and the two do a glorious
mash. His wife finds them together, and objects
strongly. Later, the wife rubs the ring in her
husband’s absence, and a fine, upstanding im-
mortal, in the person of Wallace Browlow, enters,
and another mash ensues. The husband presently
finds them together. There is some pretty, but
not especially surprising, music in this fragmen-
tary effort, and the immortals make one or two
moderately smart observations, but that is about
all.
* • •
“ Mam’zelle Nifcouche,” with Miss Juliet
Wray as the good girl of the convent school, went
well from start to finish. The new girl goes out
on her sinful burst in a manner that, in many
respects, is so exactly like the sinful burst of the
previous girl that it is hardly possible to tell one
sinful burst from another. Miss W\’s activity
and overflowing vitality eminently qualify her to
go out on a burst and make things lively while it
lasts. Unfortunately, the new star seems still to
have the remains of a bad cold on her adorable
little chest, and though her singing was very good
on the whole, and the drum solo in particular was
an admirable performance, she had to shut
down on one or two enthusiastic encores. The
rest of the cast was practically the same as it was
the last time “Nitouche” went on its demoralising
career in Sydney. Lauri added no fresh touches
to the organist, which was satisfactory, for that
mild, long-haired person was so near perfection
before that any change would almost certainly be
for the worse, and the other characters wisely
followed his example. “ Paul Jones ” was billed
Thursday and Friday, and, on Saturday,
‘ Pinafore ” is to raise its almost-forgotten head
after eight years’ absence, for the joy of a new
generation which knows not Captain Corcoran.
** * *
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) was full in every
part on Saturday night, when the Woods-Marshall
co. opened in the “ Prairie King ”—a dreadful
blood-curdler of the bear-haunted regions of the
West. It is built on the same principles as the
ordinary scalp-hunting penny-dreadful, with a ton
of tomato-colored Indians on the cover, likewise
the long-haired hero staving them off with a large
quantity of revolvers. There is not, in all its
four acts, one fragment of literary merit, except
in the part of the horse, which drops through the
broken bridge into the raging tank below. The
downfall of this noble animal, and the fearsome
splash with which he strikes the water, are
bright spots in an otherwise purposeless show.
On Saturday night King Hedley, the great Amur-
rikan actor, failed to get a hold of the ruins of the
bridge ; and went down along with the horse,
whereat the audience cheered vociferously, and
called out “encore.” The rest of the production
consists of Mr. Plimmer, as a copious villain of
the cheap and murky character who hangs round
and wants to be r-r-revenged on the King for
something not very well-defined ; Miss Harrie
Ireland as a child of the wilderness in deerskin
pants and a coat; a Mexican woman of the inco-
herent teeth-gnashing description ; two tramps
who drift in and out and cadge for liquor; a
purposeless little Jew, perpetrated in good style
by Mr. Walter Haybittle; a captive cheild, per-
petrated by nobody in particular ; and a general
tendency to rush about on horseback and fire guns
off promiscuously. Plimmer and the King wave
their artillery at each other whenever they
meet, but nothing particular happens. The
characters have no visible connection with each
other, and their chief business is to arrive suddenly
and state that the Indians may be expected at any
moment. The Prairie King himself has nothing to
recommend him save his horsemanship; he is a
stiff, dreary, awkward person, who states through
his nose that he is returning to his home in the
West. As he is then at San Franoisoo, it appears
that he is going to ride out into the Pacific Ocean
and become a Chinese mandarin. The scenery
and the horse call for unlimited commendation,
and Miss Ireland is a pleasant spectacle in the
pants, and Mr. Plimmer is a capable villain, and
the independent hair of the prairie nfbnarch is
interesting because it is so long and so liable to be
caught in the bridge, but otherwise the drama is
the Great American Horror howling in the Yose-
mite valjey, or down the canons of the Colorado,
and falling heavily into the tank as one who
refuses to be comforted.
** * *
The rather depressing “My Sweetheart,”
«t Sydney Royal, has been succeeded by a still
more depressing medley described as “Revels.”
Why it is called so this paper knoweth not.
There is a dude in it who falls on to the floor
occasionally, and a man who has had his pants
dragged off, and returns to show his legs in pink
tights, and an old, old joke about a tough fowl,
and a very ancient, moss-backed witticism about
nothing in particular, and a person who puts his
head in many, many times to say that ice-cream
is waiting inside. One or two pretty dances are
turned on here and there, but in the main
“Revels” is a sick and sorry affair which
lacks even the energy to be actively awful.
The Bulletin man began to drop off towards
the end of it, and dreamt that he was the Last
Man sitting on a cinder on top of a burnt world,
and an endless procession of skeletons went by,
60 000,000,000 abreast, all dragging their fleshless
feet through an ocean of black ashes, and champ-
ing their flesh I ess jaws, and having revels
generally. The procession went on till the whole
Caucasian race was used up, and then came
Africans, Eskimo, missing links, troglodytes, and
other sundries. And, finally, the author of the
above burlesque came along on all fours at the
extreme end of the procession, and The Bulletin
man decided that it was the right place for him,
provided the Undermost troglodyte didn’t feel
hurt at him being there. This paper desires to be
just without any undue severity, but it feels that
the joke about the tough fowl requires to be put
down with all the rigor of the law.

Monday was announced as the last night
of the “Scrap of Paper” at Sydney Criterion, so,
by the time these remarks get into print, that
house will probably have relapsed into its former
state of emptiness. Apparently, there is no great
demand for comedy just now in Sydney, which
has wandered away in pursuit of the mesmerist,
and the variety-show, and the horse that falls
into the tank, and other strange gods—at all
events, that seems the only possible explanation
why a good piece and two excellent artistes should
have retired from the scene after such a brief
season. The Daeres are now open for engagement,
and, considering their remarkable capabilities,
they should be in demand,

successful sensation in the shape of Albert Ban-
vard’s “dive from the dome,” though if Albert
wants to keep ahead of the rival sensation at Her
Majesty’s he will presently have to take a horse
up into the dome along with him, and dive with
that intelligent animal for company. The Jeru-
salem cyclorama is doing reasonably well, and a
few casual shows came out on Monday night, and
caught the passing shekel by the forelock, and
then went in again.

That old-time favorite, Mr. George Leitch,
is to take possession of Sydney Royal on the 19th
October with his stupendous spectacle, “The
Land of the Moa.” A large number of Maoris
have been imported for the production, and the
gentle savage does the old war-dances of his
fathers, and works himself up into a fearsome
state of excitement until the ancient cannibal
instinct revives, and he is liable to eat the orches-
tra at any moment. The scenery includes lakes,
mountains, volcanoes, waterfalls, and all the pic-
turesqueness which can be crowded into the avail-
able space.

The Fitzgerald Bros., after draining
Adelaide of its spare shekels and putting up a
record m that peaceful city, came along to Melb.
Exhibition Building on Saturday night. “ I he
most sensational show ever seen in Australia ” is
only a fair statement of fact concerning the deeds
of the Fitz.’s new company from Europe. Ten
turns fill the bill. Some of the smaller items
are above the average standard, whilst the four
largest have points which no colored poster could
improve upon. Two of these were not made the
most of on Saturday, Lhe stage in the centre
transept under the dome needed to stand a little
higher, with a fiercer light beating upon it. The
mid-air performances, being fully visible from afar
Rot all the credit they deserved, or nearly all, A
thrill is an immediate success, and the audience
gasps on the spot. But an appalling incident
is something for the spectators to enjoy mostly at
their leisure, a few hours after it happened. Then
they develop a burning sensation to buy another
ticket and lie thrilled again. Human nature, as
Waokford Squeers remarked, is a rum’un.
• * •
Les Freres Eclair, humorous contortionists,
seem to have discovered a few new methods of
throwing their bodies out of gear. One frere plays
the demon, and the other is a clever suggestion of
a crocodile gone mad. The whole act is a lively
exhibition of strength combined with uncanny
curves. At last, the demon stands head-
downward upon nothing. That is to say,
he places an iron extinguisher upon a pro-
perty candle, seizes the bent top of the ex-
tinguisher between his teeth, and supports himself
by means of his bite. Mahomet, the Talking
Horse, is the boss wonder of the show, considering
that he is only a lior.se. Mahomet picks up his
cues quicker, aud is probably familiar with the
different sounds of more words, than any other
trick animal of his breed. He’s a horse with a
memory. Pi< f-ssor Peart and Madame Adelina
Antonio supply the appalling incidents. Peart
is a thriller, pure and simple—nothing else. He
is hauled up to a small, shaky platform, put into
a sack, and plumped into a tank of water some 20
yards below. The tank is only Oft. x 7ft., with
6ft. depth of water in it. After emerging from
the damp sack, the Professor gets himself hauled up
to a still higher platform (on Saturday he preferred
to stop again at the lower one) and from there he
dives into the tank. There’s a lot of atmosphere
to travel through and very little water to strike.
As Peart’s rigid body slightly turns in the air
before reaching its destination, one’s heart is
brought into one’s mouth by the feat. Madame
Antonio is a charming artist in the first place, and
a thriller afterwards. Her trapeze work is the
refinement of gymnastics. Sometimes she is a
poem, sliding imperceptibly down a rope. When
Madame puts one foot in a noose and is carried
up 40 yards to a trapeze in the dome, waving a
graceful farewell to everybody meanwhile, she
might easily be mistaken for an angel ascending
to the Golden Shore. Presently Madame is seen
standing on the far-away trapeze. She falls
negligently backwards, makes two languid revolu-
tions in the air, and pops up smiling in the net
prepared to receive her. The Bulletin heard a
lawyer remark that it was “ a shame ” to let the
lady do it. Seven or eight thousand other people
didn t object. They looked pale, but happy,
k* * *
“Joseph,” at Melbourne Hoyal, seems
likely to win a large measure of religious respect
and steady support. Bible characters presented
on the stage must be convincing or ridiculous, so,
seeing that the gallery regards George Rignold’s
fine spectacle with awe and satisfaction, there can
be no doubt that himself and his brethren,
together with the Potiphars, royal Pharaoh, and
the rest of the company, have taken hold of their
lines in the right spirit and done their level
best for the reverend adapter of Holy Writ.
“Joseph” is not a lively play, but it moves im-
pressively and carries its oriental garments as
though born to the manner of long, long ago.
Also, it is loaded up with ancient and not un-
wholesome morality. Mrs. Potiphar, for instance,
makes atonement for her little indiscretion by
ultimately stabbing a conspirator in the liver, a
sort of repentance which would not hold good in
law nowadays. “Joseph’s” scenic magnificence
was duly extolled in these columns during the Syd-
ney season; wherefore a further burst of admira-
tion is not required. Every loyal Hebrew ought to
feel bound to take at least one “ liker ” at his
father Jacob, and no Gentile can conscientiously
avoid the show. Parson Haskett Smith testifies
to its Scriptural realism. The Bulletin recom-
mends “Joseph ”as a corrective to the vanity and
vexation of the present racing season,
** * *
The revival of “The Shop Girl,” decorated
with a few local verses and gags, is much about
the same brainless festival as previously. Elton
is the new Mr. Hooley, but the difference between
brisk W'lham and massive Monkhouse calls for
no particular joy or regret. Hooley, judging from
the fact that two experienced comedians of oppo-
site types have failed to make him interesting, is a
hopeless ease. On Saturday the sweet visions of
the co. will be seen again in “The Gaiety Girl,”
and then perhaps Melbourne will accept this best
of the musical comedies at its full value. When
“The Gaiety Girl” first adorned the Princess’s
stage its airy satire floated over the heads of the
masses, who had been expecting a burlesque.
Now that they have learned to digest the alleged
humor of a “ Gentleman Joe,” they are likely to
rush at “The Gaiety Girl” with open mouths,
and smack their lips as the dainty morsel goes
down.
** * *
Pastor Walters, because his Biblical play,
“Joseph of Canaan,” has been a moderate suc-
cess, shows symptoms of being stage-struck. As
he meditates going to England, we may yet hear
of him playing Joseph. Mr. Walters should “lay
to his book,” and remember how G. C. Miln gave
up a Chicago church billet of 10,000 dols. a year
“and he hasn’t been well since.” Also,"Mr.
Walters should apply an ice-cap to his swollen
head. If English and Yankee managers think
there is anything in Biblical plays, there won’t be
a single Biblical dramatic situation left unworked
in six months. The good pastor’s play contains
no memorable dialogue ; whatever success it has
made is mainly due to the idea of the thing—and
there is no copyright in mere ideas. By the time
the Rev. G.W. gets to England he will probably
find in full blast a scintillating piece entitled
“ Mrs, Potiphar’s Young Man,” or something like
that.
At Poverty Point .
Grace Palotta, according to a Melbourne paper, has a
most extraordinary liking for monkeys, and frequently
visits the local Zoo to feed and admire the animals caged
there. This announcement will probably be the means of
inducing many local johnnies and bounders to throw off
ail disguise and take to the trees again in the soulful hope
of having nuts thrown them by Palotta.
