The penultimate Gaiety Girl season at Melb. Princess’s
was florally barren; this lime the men only get the
parsley wreaths. The Gee-Gees (fern.) have suffered from
a flowery famine throughout. Bradfield, with several
unwieldy horseshoes to bis credit, and Fred Kaye, are the
most bouqeted. The latter lias an ui k iown admirer who
sends him a cabbage, made of lamp-vhade paper and tied
with pink ribbons, about twice a week.
There is a striking unanimity about the themes of the
“Gentleman Joe” songs. The keynote is “stockings.”
When the fifth Gaiety fairy comes on and sings, “ She
showed all her storking and wasn’t it shocking,” or words
to that effect, exhibiting at the same time a very modified
amount of frill to point the remark, the tiling” begins to
pall on the average spectator who has seen “Morocco
Hound,” &c. A ditty on the subject of “ socks” would
be a distinct relief. But, then, there is no comedy, only
tragedy, in socks.

Mahomet, the Fitzgeralds’ talking horse, does one trick
that is certainly above suspicion. Anyone in the audience
is allowed to write a number under 10 on a slate held by
an attendant, and the horse stamps the answer with bis
feet. The trick seems to lie in the manner of holding the
slate.
Martin Simonsen— Whose ghost occasionally walks the
streets of Melbourne—was once violinist by appointment
to the King of Denmark. There is a legend that while at
the Court of Copenhagen he knew Yoiick.
The Lucy Chambers’ monument subscription resulted
in a surplus, part of which- after the monument was paid
for—was spent in setting up a memorial bed in St. Vincent
Hospital and the rest given to the Musical Fund. This
case is almost unique. As a rule, when a popular favorite
dies the public gets drunk on its own tears at the funeral
and solemnly vows to erect a memorial pyramid, and on
the day after sobers up, and doesn’t see its way to sub-
scribe to the purchase of a little wooden cross or tin
immortelle.
It was on explorer Stanley’s recommendation that
Mark Twain placed himself under the charge of the
"Much-Travelled.” And therein is a curious coincidence.
Mark wrote to Smylhe inquiring terms, and had not
posted his letter three days when along came a note from
R.S. stating exactly what he wanted to know and com-
mencing : “Dear Mr. Clemens, —lt is so long since Archi-
bald Forties and I spent that pleasant afternoon in your
comfortable house in Hartford that you have probably
forgotten the occasion.” M.T. rejects the theory that
tiiis was a mere coincidence.

The Eclairs, whose humorous contortion act is one of
the most fetching novelties in Fitzgerald Bros.’ new show,
are both of them short, square, nuggetty men. Their
figures, in every-day garb, give no suggestion of an abnor-
mal capacity for getting out of joint. One would guess
the Eclairs to be weight-lifters, or human-pyramid sup-
porters, or anything but what they are.
Johnny Toole’s theatre has just about ceased to exist
by this time, and J.L.T., as a London actor, is probably
passing out of knowledge along with his band-box of a
house.
H. B. Conway, a fashionable-beauty actor in his time
(which wasn't so long ago, either), seems to be going the
way of Harry Montague and other “ducks of men.” At
Birmingham, tlie other week, he took laudanum,
but subsequently repented and rang for the chamber-
maid. Prompt medical treatment pulled him round.
Conway’s excuse for the opium was the severity of local
press notices of his Ned Annesly performance in “ Sowing
the Wind.” The truth is said to have been that he came
drunk upon the stage, and got his walking-ticket next
morning.
Some excellent Living Pictures have lately been shown
at church gatherings in the St. Hilda (Melb.) district,
under the management of Fred. Chamberlain, a clever
young amateur scenic artist. Considering that these
pictures are staged in school-rooms, right under the noses
of the audience, they are wonderfully effective.
Dear Bulletin, —There’s nothing unusual though
“ Sappho Smith ” may not be aware of this well-known
fact—about the case of Courtice Pounds, a church boy-
soprano who has developed into a successful musical man.
Arthur Sullivan and Alfred Cellier, as youngsters, sang
in the choirs at St. Paul’s Cathedral and Westminster
Abbey. Solomon (the “Vicar of Bray” composer) was
onetime a little glee-warbler at Evans’s supper-rooms.
Edward Lloyd was a surpliced soprano in the first place,
as likewise a dozen other well-known English vocalists.
Armes Beaumont’s voice is said to have graduated
at a tabernacle. A choir-hoy, by the way, is not a
“prodigy,” like a “calculating boy,” but merely an
ordinary result of careful tuition in music theory
and voice production. A cathedral choir-master takes
any eight-year-oid urchin who comes along, and licks
him into shape according to his natural intelligence.
Having licked the boy into shape, his master, as a rule,
proceeds to knock his voice to pieces as soon as it cracks,
instead of letting him preserve it for future use. If the
average tutor didn’t eventually ruin the young voices he
has educated, and more particularly ruin the best of
them, there would be very little room on the concert-
platform, or operatic stage, for any artists other than ex-
ohoir-boys.—Yours, Verger.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the’ appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback- the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex
tracted, so that now a beautiful comph x'on of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.

News of the Week,
Amongst the events of importance this week are ;
The continued rise in value of wool; next, the
bountiful rainfall which has turned deserts into
pastures, and, which combined with the increased
price of our staple commodity, means millions for
this colony; then the Dean case is exciting more
public interest than ever owing to latest dis-
closures ; but the most important matter to the
public is the receipt of news to the effect that
though the supply of the popular B.L. Corto
Rothschild Cigars is very short at present the im-
porters have, at great expense, secured a further
shipment, which will be to hand next week. The
B.L. Corto Rothschild are the best and most
popular 6d. cigar in Australia.*
The Wild-Cat Column .
N.b.W. ia about to float in London a £4,000,000
loan at 3 per cent., and the present signs are tliat it will
be the most successful financial operation performed up
to date by any Australian province. Premier Keid lias
enormous luck in this resp a ct. The price of money in
London is about at the lowest figure on record ; it lias
even got perceptibly lower since the Maoriland 3 per cent,
loan was put on the market a few months ago. The
£4 000,000 is wanted mostly for redemption purposes.
On’lst January, '96, £219,400 of 5 per cent, debentures
and £1,000,000 of 4 per cent. Treasury bills fall due ;
£768 000 of 5 per cent, debentures fall due on
Ist July, ’96; and another £1,000,000 of 4 per
cent. Treasury bills and £750,000 of 44 per cent,
ditto are repayable on Ist October, ’96. This makes a
total of £3,727,400, and after allowing for expenses of
flotation, the Treasurer will probably have £150,000 left
towards forest-thinning and building the Bogan bridge
at Monkey. Yet the £4,000,000 (if the loan is a success,
whioh seems tolerably certain) will cost only £120,000 a
year interest, whereas the £3,727,400 of old loans cost
over £160,000 a year. These are the good features of the
arrangement. * * •
The bad ones are as follows ; The loan is for 40
years certain; it falls due on a fixed date in the old
foolish way, instead of being an interminable loan with
power of repayment at the option of the Government
after a certain number of years. It adds £1,000,000 to
the foreign indebtedness of New South Wales, and re-
duces the local indebtedness by £750,000, so the province
i 8 so much the further away from the time when
it will be independent of the British lender. No
attempt has been made to raise any of the money locally,
so Q. H. Reid’s statement that he didn’t think it advisable
to increase the country’s foreign obligations, and that he
wanted to rely on the local market, is evidently only
windy rhetoric. Also, nothing is said about putting the
£4O 000 annually saved in interest to a sinking-fund for
the ultimate extinction of the loan, and putting that sink-
ing-fund in a place where the Treasurer can’t get at it—
if there is such a place. But, then, no one expected any-
thing of the kind, so there is no special cause for disap-
pointment. * * •
The Melbourne Board of Works has floated
another £500,000 loan locally at 4 per cent., with an 18
years’currency. The price realised was £lO4 4s. 7d., so
that the board'really gets the money at about £3 12s. per
cent., allowing for the profit on repayment. If N.S.W.
Government gets its S per cent, loan in London at £96
net—and considering the heavy charges therefor stamp-
duty commission, &c., this will be a high figure—it will
pay rather more than £3 4s. 6d. per cent., including the
final loss on repayment, and will also have to pay con-
siderable annual charges for remitting interest, manage-
ment in London, &c. Considering that the province of
NSW offers so much better security than a mere local
board, and that it gives 40 years’ currency instead of 18,
it seems certain from these figures that Premier Reid
could ha e got his loan almost as cheaply in Australia as
in London, and the after-savings in expenses would pro-
bably have made it quite as cheap—possibly even a shade
cheaper. Also, the dividends would have been subject to
income-tax, while the London divs., owing to the stupid
and unpatriotic preference given to the foreign lender in
the Income Tax Bill, are not. Further local flotation
would have furnished an investment for much of
the bank reserves which have now to be kept idle for
want of liquid securities, so that it would have been
a direct cause of prosperity in New South Wales ; and, in
the local market, the money could have been more con-
veniently raised in instalments as it was wanted, whereas
bv the present arrangement the country loses about
£50,000 by paying interest on money borrowed long
before there was any necessity for it. Altogether, the
time Was so opportune that a Treasurer with any sem-
blance of pluck would at least have made the attempt to
borrow locally.
* • • •
The Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
has just finished its quinquennial investigation, and re-
ports a cash surplus of £236.870. Of this amount
£5l 311 is to be set aside as a reserve to cover possible
depreciation, £63.103 is carried forward, £5887 is written
oft securities, and £105,896 is divided among the policy-
holders. These provisions make a reserve for deprecia-
tion of nearly £3 ss. per cent, on the investments of the
Society. The A.M.P. has a reserve fund equal to £3 4s. per
cent or just about the Colonial Mutual’s figure. The
National Mutual has a reserve of £2 4s. per cent., the
Maoriland Government Department £2 per cent, (though
in its case most of the funds are invested in State
securities, and the mortgage item, where the losses mostly
come in, is very limited, therefore its reserve is much
belter than it looks). The Mutual of Victoria, according
do last balance-sheet, had a reserve of 13s. 4d. per cent. ;
while the Widows’ Fund had only 6s. 3d. to cover possible
■depreciation on each £lOO of assets. The Mutual Life
Association’s last balance-sheet shows no reserve what-
ever. The Colonial Mutual has thus taken about the most
solid precautions of the lot, and, in these lamentable
dimes, it has done wisely.
During the five years the funds have increased
from £1,050,573 to £1,793,628-an advance of three-
.quarters of a million ; and the ratio of expenses to pre-
miums has been reduced by nearly 6 per cenl. The bonus
as quite as large as could be expected considering that
the Colonial Mutual charges the lowest premium rates in
Australia. During the 21 years of its existence the
society has paid £1,405,000 in claims, and the total
amount of its present insurances is 10J millions. Alto-
gether, the report is highly satisfactory, save perhaps in
one respect. The Colonial Mutual apparently still bases
its calculations on the assumption that its funds will earn
in the future an average of 4 per cent, interest. All the
other Australasian societies, save the A.M.P., estimate on
the same, or on an even higher basis, and the fact that the
funds actually realise a good deal over 5 per cent, makes
the calculation look absolutely safe. Nevertheless, there
has been of late a dull, sickening thud in interest-rates,
and it seems just possible that the 4 p a r cent, estimate
may leave a somewhat narrow margin a few years hence.
The A.M.P., which adopted the same st andard I ill very
recently, is now getting down by degrees to 3.,, and the
other offices should do likewise. It will he much cheaper
to make the descent by slow instalments now than to do
it all in one act later on.
* * » •
The Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment
and Guarantee Co. (Sydney), reports, in a pained tone of
voice, that, though the paid-up capital lias been increased
during the past year from £125,000 to £137,000, nothing
else of a cheering nature has happened. The net loss
during the year has been £7951. The C.F.M.I. and G.
Co., like most of its kind, is choked with unsaleable free-
hold properties, hut it is arranging to sell them in a lump
to the Equitable Building Society of Brisbane for disposal
on the lottery dodge principle. Should this transaction
come off it will be clear of its present difficulties, and
then it proposes to make a fresh start and walk a chalk-
line for the future.
* * * *
The Victorian Government is importing a
Yankee tobacco-growing expert and a Maoriland cheese-
making expert, to give instruction to the Victorian
farmers, with a view to improving the quality of the local
produce. It is also importing the best varieties of sugar
beet seed, and is sending an expert round to give
information as to the most profitable melhodsof growing.
At present low prices there is little to be made by tear-
ing crops out of the ground for export to England, but if
by helping along the beet industry it is possible to save
th a £500,000 per annum which goes out of Victoria to
pay for imported sugar, the province will he much the
richer. Toe local market is always more profitable than
the one at the other end of the globe, and the 'cry
rational object of the Vic. Government is to work it for
ail it is worth.

How J. Bull falls in. An Australian mine (a
really valuable asset) is sold to Scotch syndicate for just
under £30,000 cash. New manager of the haw-haw type
takes charge. Spends a few thousands in well-meaning
endeavors to woo the sullen metal. Finally, in disgust,
the mine is sold back to original owner for about £2500.
New owner finds that on the plates has been left enough
stuff to pan out £5OOO. So he is 12500 and a mine to the
good, to say nothing of his profit on the first transaction.

A novelty in Trades Unionism lias been
furnished by the Melb. bootmakers, w r ho have, on their
own initiative, not that of the employers, agreed to a
reduction of wages—this to enable the union factories to
sell more cheaply and thus take away some of the trade
from the non-union shops. But it remains to be seen
whether this kind of unionism can’t he practised just as
successfully by the non-unionists.

S.A. Opposition-leader Downer got hit by bis
own boomerang when he moved a test-vote in the State
Advances Bill, to strike out all reference to the Savings
Bank. He carried his motion by 22 votes to 19; hut the
Attorney-General at once announced that the Govt, meant
to go on with the Bill and embody in it the creation of a
State Bank.
• * * •
To many criminals, handy with the graver, it
will lie galling news that hitherto no law has been in force
in Victoria to make forgery of stamps an offence.
Operators, viewing a lost opportunity, must feel some-
thing like the Yankee skipper in the early days of Maori-
land trading. Noting the rugged conformation of that
country, and learning of the absence of all venomous
reptiles, the toiler of the deep cried: “What! No
snakes? That’s rough on the sarpint. I’ll bring some
along next trip.”

Recently The Bulletin urged the establish-
ment in N.S.W. of a permanent fund to relieve the
widows and families of the victims of fatal mining acci-
dents—on tlie ground, mainly, that private charity is
only forthcoming when some phenomenal accident hap-
pens, and that Lite one-man accident passes unnoticed.
Mines Minister Smith says he proposes to convene shortly
a conference of miners, similar to those of fruitgrowers
and rabbit-plagued people lately held. He adds that he
will ask this conference to suggest a scheme.

* * » *
At Melb. Federal Coffee Palace Co.’s 20th
half-yearly meeting Chairman Godfrey once more re-
marked to the shareholders: —“ Until the palace is a fully
licensed hotel, there is no hope of your getting any return
for your capital. It is only again going over the old
ground to explain that nothing whatever can be lost by
the granting of the licenses to the large coffee-palaces.
Wines and spoils— out of which the co. makes no profit-
are consumed in the dining-rooms and in the bed-rooms
now to a very considerable extent, which it is impossible
to prevent; nor would it he wise to do so, as one-half of
the business now done would be driven away were the
directors to attempt to disallow the consumption of drink
on the premises.”

Mr. J. S. Alloutt, the Queensland Govt,
freezing, canning and curing meat-expert, who lias just
completed a three years’ engagement, will shortly start
works of his own at Broadsound, C.Q., where he has
seven shareholders who are stock-owners, and can supply
150,000 cattle. His speciality will he mess beef—the
choice parts of the beast—which will he pul in brine and
exported to London for sale. For 25 years Mr. Allcutt
was in the service of the Armour Co., of Chicago, which
puts through haif-a-million of cattle and a million pigs
annually.

ing scheme did not go with the boom the Australian pub-
lic has been led to believe. The underwriters, when the
mail left, were st uck with about 60 per cent, of the shares,
and were sore ab 'Ut it. Ashcroft’s process to solve the
sulphide problem is expected to be taken more readily.
Small works to treat 000 tons of B.H. sulphides are
erected in London, and some strong men are waiting to
handle the scheme.
t * * *
Said that the crowd behind the Australian
Smelting Co. aims at a monopoly of the B.H. sulphide
mines. A London man “in” talks about them having
four millions to back their project. Up to dale, the
Smelting Go’s agents havepicked up 50,000 Junctions and
some 30,000 Block iO’s.
* * * •
The writer learns from London that the Cool -
gardie boom has fairly started again ; new companies are
going off at the rate of three or four a day, the shares are
readily taken, and J. Bull is buying good and bad alike,
apparently indifferent as to whether the mines are good
or bad. The greater the boom the bigger the collapse,
and that of Coolgardie will be a snorter.
* * * •
The Auckland (M.L.) gold-boom has now
reached that frenzied state when claims are pegged out
by the dozen, the pegs overlapping each other in the most
alarming manner, and before the ground is surveyed, or
the leases granted, or any prospecting done, companies
are floated and the shares rushed up to a big premium.
Someone will be left to hold the baby very shortly, and a
really stupendous infant it will be for a city of Auckland s
size to tackle.
♦ ♦ ♦ •
B.H. Proprietary is still under a heavy cloud,
despite the payment of a bonus additional to the 100th
div. The fire in Block 11 continues to smoulder ; and, in
consequence of the persistent sinking of the floor of the
big open-cut (beneath which the fire broke out) the Govt,
mines inspector has ordered work there to close. Partly
on account of fhe dislocation of operations caused by
this fire, suitable ore for milling is not immediately avail-
able, and so the amalgamating plant was shut down last
week. Meantime the mine underground continues to be
raked for smelting lead ores.
Central (8.H.) made a profit of .£19,923 last
half-year, of which £11,052 was distributed in divs. To
produce this result 13,731 tons of ore were sold and 12,514
tons were smelted. But products unrealised and sums yet
to be drawn will probably bring the total to about £21,000
or £22,000, so that a profit of approximately 17s. per ton
has been made. It must, however, be remembered that
the co. has been running two smelting furnaces for
several months, but it has now been found impossible to
continue doing so.
Junction (8.H.) still develops well; though it
does strike one that the directors are most careful that
the public shall know the fact—a solicitude very rare in
these days. Still, the lode, opened up at 550 ft. in new
country, is worth talking about ; it is, besides being big,
one of the best-looking bodies now showing along the line.
Large parcels of these shares have been bought up lately
for foreigners.
A lot Of 523 tons of North B.H. sulphides has
been tried on the South mill wit h very satisfactory results.
The stuff is easy of treatment, and gave 170 tons concen-
trates, assaying in bulk 69 per cent, lead, Boz. silver, and
7 per cent. zinc.
As against the higher return for the “dry ” and
lead-smelting ores at Broken Hill must, however, be put
the much higher cost of winning these classes by com-
parison with the sulphides. General-manager Adams, of
the Central, shows that while it cost only 7s. lid. per ton
to raise the ordinary sulphides last half-year, it cost 14s.
Id. per ton to get out the carbonates, and 16s. Bd. the
dry ore.
Central (8.H.) proposes to begin the erection of
a concentrating mill. By the deed, this co. is to be
allowed tor any improvements made up to £5OOO in the
event of the completion of the purchase by the “ Ameri-
can ” syndicate. The first term of the offer expires on
28th Oct., by the way.

Gilgunnia field, N.S.W., is without a battery.
Upwards of 3000 tons are at grass, and that sent to Cobar
arid Sydney went from 3 to 6oz. per ton. Here is a chance
for an enterprising man with an idle 10 or 16-head battery - .
The miners say they would not mind £1 a ton to start
wiih. Good water-area and two or three large tanks in
the locality might be utilised.
♦ ♦ ♦ ♦
The New Bushman’s mine, Parkes (N.S.W.),
has started its 20-head battery. ’Tis said a bona-fide offer
of £68,000 has been refused. A good mine undoubtedly,
and £68,000 is a good price.
* ♦ * *
The Drysdale mine (N.S.W.) has had another
sensational crushing—ls tons sent to Clyde works gave
over 900 oz. gold. The owners have not treated this mine
well. They have divided upwards of £30,000 in divs., and
spent over £lO,OOO in the treatment, at Wallaroo and
elsewhere, of about 1500 tons of ore, yet have done no
development or prospecting work. They are at last pro-
viding a plant, and it is to he hoped the balance of profit
from this crushing will go for prospecting, notin a div.,
or the mine will soon be in trouble.

Pambula. Rich ore still coming out.

» * • •
Great Boulder —last crushing, 345 tons, 18790 z.
Not up to the average, but quite good enough for any-
thing if it lasts ; though it is strange to have so big a
drop after so many yarns to the effect that the next
crushings were going to be bigger than ever. In mining,
however, it is always the next crushing that is going to
make the cake. Z"b. Lane tells a Ooolgardie P^P® 1-
that after examining the mine in ’93 he told bis friends in
England that it was “ good enough,” and his judgment
was supported by that of Begelhole, “ who is said to be
one of the best mining experts in the world.” Itis.no
doubt, somewhat painful to he reminded of the fact that
there were quite a number of other Ooolgardie mines con-
sidered good enough by one or other, if not both, these
gentlemen. What, for instance, has become of the
Empress of Ooolgardie, Cashman’s, the Great Ooolgardie,
&c., &c. ? True, the Great Boulder looked the sickest of
the lot when the writer was out there, but it is unreason-
able to expect so great a transformation to repeat itself.

Neglected Wood’s Point (Vic.) owns a promising
mine in the Hope, developing the diorite dyke on the
Morning Star Hill, one of the rich pots of the aforesaid
field in its famous days. Then, from a few reefs above
the water, a million oz. gold were taken, and the drill has
proved the reefs to recur every 60 or 100 ft. iu the dyke.
Hope’s last crushing went ISdwts., and the stone now
stamping will, it is said, go over the ounce. “Johnny”
\\ allace owns the hulk of the shares. There are 10,000
paid-up and 20,000 contributing called up to Bs. Sd.
Directors are compiling a reserve—hence the postpone-
ment of divs. Another seemingly good show on the Hill
is the New Morning Star, which will not reach the Hope
stage of development lor some months.

♦ • • •
Mt. Lyell stocks are now the chief bite in
Melb. Shares in the big mine are well on the way to £6—
at which figure the market-value of the property would
he a million and a-half ; sanguine holders are whispering
in each other’s ears they will see double the money—the
anticipation being (bat more foreign capital will be avail-
able for this stock. If the writer were a holder he
wouldn’t lock up his scrip till that happened. Big smelt-
ing plant now in course of erection, and until that la
working the question of divs. must be adjourned. And it
must not lie forgotten that the Mt. Lyell pyritic-smeltlng
plan is a pure experiment.
Syndicate blocks fringing the aforesaid pro-
perty, or within cooee of it, are having a sympathetic
“ buck.” North and South Lyells have had their spurt,
and last Friday King Lyells and Queen Lyells jumped
from £l6 to £33 and £3 to £9 ss. respectively. Out of
King’s one Melh. man has cleared £5OOO.

Rutherglen district is now the principal Vio.
mining “ dart.” All available country near or within
smelting distance of well known leads snapped up.
M.L.C. “Johnny ” Wallace pioneered Ruther-
glen district, where he has made, and also dropped, heaps
of coin. Chiltern Valley United, turning out 1500 oz.
monthly, is his prize show, only a quartette of small
shareholders having a cut at the rich divs.
» • • •
Re the announced sales of Johnson’s Reef mines,
the writer is assured there is “nothing in it.” When
approached the directors told Lansell’s ostensible London
buyer that the shareholders would chew over a £lOO,OOO
offer, and he retaliated with £75,000. No consideration.
Johnson’s is a property, judging from past performances,
that may come again any day. Eleven years elapsed
between its first and second div.-eras, and up to date it has
returned £lll2s. 9d. on each of the 28,000 shares paid-up
to £2 17s. 6d. In No. 2 mine, with another 100 ft. driving,
they expect to out the Princess Dagmar reef, now show-
ing a little gold. , , ,
A British buyer, piloted by George Lansell, has,
it is said, annexed a couple of promising Bendigo mines,
and he is also nibbling at the United Devonshire and
New Chum Railway, where, by the way, the heat in the
2878 ft. level has caused suspension of work. Expected
the difficulty will be got over by drawing a supply of cool
air from the' ladder-shaft, which, for that purpose, will
be boxed in from 1200 ft.

Society.
The situation this week in regard to the
eternal Dean problem is that Want still says that
Salomons told him that Meagher told him that
Dean told him that he had administered the
poison. Also, Salomons says that he did tell
Want, and that he also told Pilcher, Simpson, and
Cecil Stephen ; and Pilcher, Simpson, and Cecil
Stephen are all apparently agreed Jthat Salomons
told them. Meanwhile, Dean still asserts most
emphatically that he didn’t tell Meagher, and
Meagher still declares that he didn’t tell
Salomons, and the fact that Salomons told three
other people besides Want doesn’t strengthen his
case in the least, seeing that the statement that
Meagher told him still rests wholly on Salomons’s
own assertion. Also, even it could be proved abso-
lutely that Meagher told Salomons, which is
obviously impossible, the question would promptly
knock up against another hopeless dead-
wall in the impossibility of proving that Dean
ever told Meagher, On the other hand, there is
no possible means of proving that Dean didn’t tell
Meagher or that Meagher didn’t tell Salomons,
and even if the parties yell at each other on the
present basis for 40,000 years they will never get
any “forrarder.” Prolmbly the most effective
way to settle the question is for the principal dis-
putants to sit down in a warm place, each with a
lump of sugar before him, and the oner whose lump
of sugar gets a fly on it fir.-t to take the pool.
* * •
. The Jaxt trouble is that a certain chemist who
is alleged to have been worried into a confession
by his conscience, assisted by a clergyman, is said
to have told something in 30 foolscap pages to
Attorney-General Want, and Want told a de-
tective, and then the force went out and solemnly
Crick, Meagher and Dean for “con-
spiracy.” The statement was made on Friday
evening, and the arrest took place on Saturday,
and then for 10 solid hours every newsboy in
Sydney bellowed the information till he almost
dropped from sheer exhaustion. The speed with
which the Government moved in this case, when
it was a matter of proving that an influential
.fudge had been right after all, contrasts remark-
ably with its extreme slowness in the Butler con-
spiracy, which was a case of proving that an in-
fluential Judge had been very far wrong indeed.
Iso, the authorities seemingly did their level
best to “ rub it in ” to their ancient enemy, Crick,
by issuing warrants instead of proceeding by
criminal summonses which, for all proper pur-
poses, would have been just as effective ; and
a vigorous protest was made by a Crown Law
official against the granting of bail, in which case
Crick ami Meagher would have been locked up
until their trial. Bail is ordinarily only refused
in capital cases and when there is supposed to be
serious risk of the accused bolting. The three
accused,Jor whom plenty of bondsmen were forth-
coming, were, however, all bailed out, and for
hours after their release excited citizens kept
arriving in cabs to offer more bail. And now
Dean is a bigger draw’ on the Mosmah’s Bay line
than ever,' and the Wallaby is loaded to the
water’s edge almost every trip, and every time it
conies into the wharf a frantic crowd points with a
grimy finger at the alleged conspirator at the
wheel, and fells his mate : “That’s ’im.”
Premier iteid was at Parramatta on Saturday
afternoon (Attorney-General Want was “away
fishing,” as usual), and is there alleged to have
accidentally learnt of the arrests. “He turned as
white as a ghost,” says the veracious chronicler.
Now, Mr. Reid may carry about with him the
useful capacity of turning as white as a ghost
whenever it suits him to play the innocent; or the
bull-headed members of his Ministry may have
manoeuvred to keep him in ignorance of the next
move in the game for fear his love of compromise
and dislike of violent measures would thwart their
plans. As Premier Reid doesn’t seem able to
blush, it is satisfactory to know that he can turn
white when it suits him.
* # #
The impression received by a number of
those who listened to Salomon’s now historic
speech, in reply to attorney Meagher, was that he
wished it clearly understood by people generally
that he was on intimate terms with the Almighty.
That the Deity and little Salomons would uphold
the right and ensure justice being done. That
the Deity need not feel in the slightest degree
uneasy, as it always had and would receive the
recognition and support of “ Sir Julian.” Also,
that Julian Salomons was indirectly the founder
of Christianity.
• # #
More than once in his Leg. Council speech
Pilcher, Q.C., asserted there “was only one
person in the world who knew whether Geo. Dean
was guilty or innocent, and that person was Geo.
Dean himself.” That, however, was only another
way of asserting Dean’s guilt, for if he were inno-
cent, Dean, for one, would know of the fact, and
the guilty person (or persons) would also know of
it. But, as according to the learned Q.C., Dean
alone in the world knows, therefore Dean must
be guilty. Q.E.D. “ Possum ” should ask Pilcher,
Q. for an apology.
* # •
Salomons, Q.C., regarded Meagher’s al-
leged confession of Dean’s supposed guilt as a
great and awful secret, which weighed him down
and tormented him in his dreams; and yet this
tremendous secret he related in as secret a manner
to his son-in-law, to C. B. Stephen, Pilcher,
R. E. O’Connor, Attorney-General Want, and
Chief Justice Darley. Despite this, and the fact
that all the gentlemen mentioned are married
men, the Q.C. professes to be astonished how the
“ rumor ” got about ? “ What is known to more
than one is no longer a secret.”
* * *
The Salomons - Meagher recriminatory
“Dean” speeches in N.S. W. Assembly put the
gallery hands on their mettle. On each occasion
the sanctum was inordinately crowded, and, as the
speeches were reported verbatim, the task of tak-
ing a creditable note with such confined elbow-
room was anything but easy. Salomons, Q.C., is
a “ teasing ” speaker. He may start a sentence
very slowly, and before it closes fly into an im-
passioned appeal of 180 or 200 words a minute.
• # #
About the only clear-cut crystal from the
Salomons-Meagher-Dean controversy:—“l came
to the conclusion that, whether Mr. Meagher said
this thing or that thing, Dean never said anything
to justify Mr. Meagher in saying it. . . 1
know as much about the case as my hon. friend,
or, perhaps, more, and I have formed the opinion’
as I have held all through, that Dean was not
guilty of the charge laid against him, and that
the evidence given, either at the trial or before the
Commission, signally failed to establish a case
which would justify any jury in convicting him.”
—C. E. Pilcher, Q.C., M.L.C.

The sanctified Gov. Buxton is alleged to
have invited General Booth to put up at Adelaide
Government House during his approaching visit.
This is a sign of how things will be during the
reign of the holy beer potentate. A cold atmos-
phere of prayer, exhortation and weak tea is
already settling down upon the land, and the dark
stiadow of Booth’s vast red nose lies heavy upon
Adelaide. A pale Governor in side whiskers who
calls society “ beloved brethren,” and asks if it is
prepared for death, will cause a lot of disloyalty.

Bishop Goe and the Melb. R.C. clergy are
having a newspaper battle over the absurd ques-
tion as to whether Card, Newman “believed” in
the Pope’s infallibility, or merely “accepted ” the
dogma obediently ? And of such is the Kingdom
of Heaven, we are given to understand. When
these rev. teachers get to the Throne of Grace
their first care will be to find Jonah and ask him
if he really thinks he was swallowed by the whale.
* # *
An interesting episode occurred at Melb.
Govmentouse the other day when “society”
rushed the establishment to enter its name in
Gov. Brassey’s visiting-book just opened for the
big event. Two ladies, who were struggling for
conspicuous and commanding positions, suddenly
clashed and, with little squeals and passionate
remarks, began to pull wool, and claw, and tear
up each other’s millinery. The trouble arose over
a question of propriety, or precedence, or some-
thing, and was for a time continued with much
spirit. Jhe ladies finally gathered up their
rags and fled, breathing vengeance and weeping
bitter tears, bassiety is understood to be much
put about over the affair. The best people, it
seems, only excuse such exhibitions in the rush
for supper at gubernatorial balls.

Poor, busted Melbourne ! It adores not
Brassey, but his Brass—the golden-calf of J. Bull.
Photo, by Elliott & Fry, London.
FOWELL-BUXTON, South Australia’s new
Evangelical Governor.
Infuriated Good Templars of the whisker-
less sex write passionately to Melb. papers because
at a recent “ Vic. Alliance” tea-and-toast banquet
the order of the rush for the luncheon was
“gentlemen first, ladies to follow.” The angry
correspondents describe this as an insult to the
pettiooated Reohabites, almost sufficient to drive
them to drink, and demand to know why the sexes
were not sent up together. Needless to remark,
the Victorian Alliance is not that sort of alliance—
it is opposed to mixed bathing, and mixed drink-
ing, also mixed feeding. It is The Bulletin’s
opinion that the new order was wisely adopted
after due consideration had been given to the
voracity of the average teetotal female.
• # «
By the Order-in-Council in connection
with Vic. police sexagenarian officers, Chief Com-
missioner Chomley retains office for a further
term. There seems to be a difficulty in finding
a suitable man to succeed him, although, as there
is a general desire that he should continue in
command, no big effort was made. Several other
sexagenarians are also being retained. Mean-
while, the rank-and-file grumble at the block in
promotions caused thereby.

The real crux of the charge against the
three Melbourne constables “ potted ” in the
Synnot case lay in the precise part they played in
working up evidence. According to statements
made, and documents produced at the enquiry,
Constable Coffey got permission on specific terms
to gi\e certain information vc the alleged house of
ill-fame, at which Mrs. Synnot was falsely stated
to have been staying. Inspecting-Superintendent
Sad lei r was directed by Chief Commissioner
Chomley to hear what Coffey had to tell, and
if there was nothing objectionable in it, from a
police point of view, lie was to have permission
to impart his knowledge to solicitor Michie. This
permission was duly accorded. But Coffey went
much further than his authority warranted. He
Quilty to come into the case as well,
and Quilty subsequently got Coghlan. For which,
if any, of the other witnesses these men were res-
ponsible has not been clearly shown. It would
considerably help to settle the position of the dis-
missed policemen, either as scapegoats or scoun-
drels, if particulars of their records in the force
were published. For it is with men as with
horses, the best or worst view is taken of them
according to their previous performances.

Some of the Melb. cycling women are
having costumes that are by no means so slack in
the tit as are the orthodox “knickers.” Depend
upon it, the baggy pants are only the bashful and
tentative stage of the knicker movement. They
will get closer and closer in fit, and the day is not
tar distant when women with “ good legs ” (every
woman thinks she has good legs) will cycle in
tights. Beyond that, it is undesirable to see at
present.

At a meeting of the members of Sydney’s
most fashionable Congregational Church, the other
night, a resolution was passed directing the atten-
ion of the Council of Churches to the wicked car-
toons which have lately appeared in The Bulletin
on the subject of prayer-for-rain. These cartoons
the ignorant and ridiculous persons in question

A most pathetic question came up for dis-
cussion at a recent meeting of the North Melt).
AT THE CHURCH DOOR.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yea, madarne, draw your skirts aside
To let that “dreadful creature ” pass;
For what have you, a new-made bride,
In common with the “ fallen ’’ class?
At those who live in “ open shame,”
Unwedded wives, you look askance;
Yet how much better, save in name,
Your “ mariage de convenance ”?
Perchance they sinned through love at first,
That passion you have never known ;
A ou hold them now as things accurst,
And cast the Pharisaic stone.
And if they sell themselves for bread,
For shelter—aye, for greed alone,
Can you, unblushing, lift your head
And swear their sin is not your own ?
You have the sanction of the law —
Your loveless contract blessed in church ;
You walk discreetly that no flaw
Your reputation may besmirch.
Yet, who are you to utter slurs,
And yon poor harlot to despise?
The diff’rence’twixt your fall and hers
Is but —the value of the prize 1
Melb-
A. V. GREENWOOD.

ST. ALBAN’S ESTATE AND STUD
DISTRIBUTION.
The date is fast approaching for the drawing of
the above, and it behoves all those who desire an
interest to lose no time in applying for shares, as
the list is rapidly filling up, the demand from
syndicates in the various colonies being very great
within the last few days, while it is fully antici-
pated that the limit will be reached within the
next week or two. This famous estate is well
known and acknowledged to be the most complete
establishment of its kind in Australia, and the
lucky holder of the winning number of this, the
first prize, would find it a rather profitable invest-
ment for .£1. The other 129 prizes include such
famous sires as Trenton, Eiridspord, Robinson
Crusoe, Strathmore, Bill of Portland, a number of
fashionably-bred mares, colts, fillies, and such far-
famed racehorses as Carnage, Havoc, Wallace,
Challenger, Steadfast, Bradford, Nada, Merman,
&o., &o., most of the latter being engaged in the
forthcoming carnivals at Caulfield and Fleming-
ton. The drawing is definitely fixed for Thurs-
day, the 24th inst., and being under the guidance
and supervision of Mr. George Adams, of Tatter-
sail’s, no better guarantee is necessary that the
whole affair will fie carried out in a straight and
careful manner.*
John Burns is now a father, a boy having
been born to his wife after a long period of matri-
mony. Between the London County Council, the
House of Commons, the Parliamentary Committee
of the Trades Congress, his bitter enemies, and
the baby, John’s hands will be full for the next
few years. !

Presbytery, re the question of appointing laymen

* # *
A Westralian jeweller and mining-pioneer
died lately, leaving a young and handsome widow
and some children. By his will, he directed that
the widow was to have the life-use of his estate,
and the children the remainder; but if she re-
married she was to lose everything. She did re-
marry, like every other handsome young widow
at any rate, she went through a form of marriage
in S.A. with her deceased husband’s brother.
Such marriages are illegal both in S.A. and W. A.,
and the Westralian Supreme Court has held that
the lady is still a widow, and entitled to enjoy the
income from her late husband’s estate. Which
decision, as virtually upsetting the tyrannous
terms of the will, is most satisfactory.

The latest re New Australia ; “ After two
years’ trouble, disappointment and bard labor—
soapless, tealess, breadless, meatless the
colonists are happy in that they’ve got maize and
vegetables enough to keep them from starving.
They are 1000 miles from the coast and cannot
grow coffee —the only payable export. And
around they see an ignorant, idle population
regarding theiv with half-curious, half-jealous
interest.”

The practice of hiring out “harmless”
lunatics from the local asylum has become popular
at Sunbury, Vic. One local family engaged an
amiable, matronly crank for her “keep, ’ and
were delighted with their bargain for a while, as
the “ patient ” would start working at day-
light, and go on without pause for rest or
refreshments unless turned off at the main. But
one day, when washing, she dreamily dipped a
billy of boiling water out of the copper, and
poured it over the baby sitting near, and then
cheerfully proceeded to grind the infant on a
washing-board. Wherefore, the cheap idiot is in
temporary disfavor at Sunbury.

The Anglican chaplain at Melb. cemetery
lately ventilated a galling grievance he has long
suffered at the hands of a man who runs Govt,
funerals by contract. The parson in question has
also a general retainer, under which he is called
upon to read the burial service over all Protestants
buried at State expense. Of late he has found,
however, that he was frequently being called upon
to perform professionally over Govt, corpses of
the R.C. persuasion, the religion of deceased
having been altered in the necessary documents
by that funeral contractor—by which act the con-
tracting chaplain was duped into piloting Romish
souls to Heaven ; whereas Romish souls are not
included in his schedule of souls, nor in the essence
of the contract he has, but count as extras, as it
were, and should be paid for as such. The funeral
contractor was fined for his unseemly conduct, but
the contracting chaplain gets nothing for those
extras. The Bulletin sees for him no resource
but to go to the graves and recall his consoling
remarks, and withdraw the service he performed
by reading it backwards.

In Maoriland it is illegal to sell drink to a
single Maori woman, or to one who is married to
a man of her own race, but a Maori woman who is
wedded to a white man can have all the drink she
wants. The lady in that case becomes a sort of
naturalised drunk, presumably.

Last week’s alleged leper is a Sydney book-
canvasser.
m * *
A Sydney suburban chemist advertised
for “a junior assistant.” One who app'ied was
asked by the pharmacist whether lie could cook a
chop and make a bed. He found only one bed
which, from its appearance, had evidently been
occupied jointly by the boss and the previous
assistant. Also, the junior was apparently sup-
posed to cook and wash-up generally. The pay
was 15s. a week, with one night off. What a
prospect! Serve your time, earn a pharmacy
certificate, and you may be able to earn 15s.
weekly as druggery rouseabout. Nowadays, the
drug in the market is the druggist.

The New Woman is an ugly woman, who
wears ugly clothes, and rides a bicycle on the
wrong side of the road.
• # •
Courtship, Betrothal, and afterwards
Yearn, Yarn, Yawn.
Distinction in dress for the sexes
The pathway of virtue is blocking,
A gown simply teases and vexes
The soul that is swayed by a stocking :
Are “ they ” shapely, or lean and contorted ?
But the girls to such secrets are stickers,
Thus the plans of reformers are thwarted
By a foolish objection to knickers.
The white linen dickey which covers
The breast of a damozel pretty
Can’t chasten the thoughts of her lovers,
If the coat of the charmer be petti.
The hot flame of passion, still burning,
Alternately blazes or flickers,
Till she crushes their amorous yearning
By patting her limbs into knickers.
Long skirts are undoubtedly rather
Seductive to masculine feelings,
How oft near a staircase we gather
Expectant of spicy revealings ;
Or gaze with expression sardonic
At the gambols of gauzy high-kickers,
Though our love would be pure and platonic
If the ballet were put into knickers.

Ladies who value a clear skin and fresh
complexion, and who appreciate the possession of
clean, white teeth and a sweet breath, should
follow the example of their European sisters and
indulge in the use of Amykos. After being out
in the hot sun, or on a dusty road, nothing is more
cooling or refreshing than a little Amykos
sprinkled in the water in which you lave your
face and mouth. One does not require to be
using it all day long, but those who have experi-
enced its benefits say that the morning ablutions
are certainly not complete without it. Purchase !
a sample bottle from your chemist.*'

My Dear Moorabinda—
Manly Flower Show was not quite the usual
success, though, considering the season, the dis-
play made was a tin-pot marvel in its way. It
wan rather bad luck for the management that,
while the drought made even a spray of “ tickey ”
bush a priceless thing, it should also break up in
to spoil the show-attendance into the bargain,
ihe flowers were not seen to as great advantage
as m the Pavilion—the Aquarium hall is so close
2n a nd J bOXy ’ for one and > when the room was
"'led, you couldn't see the stalls properly. Also,
the odor of dying Australian wild flowers is not
the most pleasant thing in the world.
Mrs. J. Fallon’s stall was a very clever floral
model of the R.C. College, Manly. The pretty
Warnngah stall had two floral bikes for a trade-
mark, so why the stallholders didn’t seize the
opportunity of dispensing their beautiful wares,
clad—l mean the girls—in “ rational ” costume, is
another mystery. The reason can’t be that the
“ rational ” woman is too new for “Our Village ”
fa* X saw two biking by the pier, attired in
fladder-hke knickers, long-tailed coats, and hats
like felt periwinkles.
The Palace stall was an imposing floral edifice,
which rather swamped Mrs. Freehill’s Dutch-house
1 airy Bower” with waratahs on the roof, and
damsels in gowns the color of water-melon flesh
to sell the perishable goods therein. “ Esperanza ”
stall was a shady sort of nook, and the aquarium
tank was a study in those vegetables which toil
not neither do they spin, combined with a floral
bridge and a merely spectacular gondola. The
Acting-Governoress, with red roses in her bonnet
and accompanied by Miss Darley, dressed in the
color of: ti-tree bark, and squired by Captain
Dangar, opened the show smoothly—the only
hitches being that the second-prize bouquet was
handed to her instead of the first, by mistake.
By the way, anew juvenile industry was nipped
in the bud at the M.F.S.—small girls and boys
abstracting bouquets from the stalls and selling
them for their own profit! Young Australia
evidently believes in every child being its own
charity !
This yarn—alleged to be the true and ghastly
experience of a Sydney man of non-spookist ten-
dencies recently returned from ’Ome—l would like
to dedicate to a certain, learned Judge. It’s a
long story, an nature!, and wants turned-down
lights and hollow tones to make your flesh creep
properly ; and as you can’t get exactly that effect
with pen and paper, it’s best to condense. Mr.
Asterisk, the Sydney man aforesaid, was stopping
with soi;ne duke or other in an ancient baronial
hall, and as he was brushing his bald head on the
first night, preparing for dinner, he heard a tap at
the door. Opening it, he saw on the mat a
Johnnie in full evening dress who begged him to
exeuse being disturbed, but he was wanted directly
in the Blue Library, or the Purple Coal-hole or
somewhere of the sort. Mr. Asterisk followed
him at once, but was surprised to find the sur-
roundings outside his door quite different to when
he went in (it was before dinner, recollect), and
When at last his guide threw open a door and re-
yealed a pretty girl whom he had never seen
before, asleep, on a sofa, he wasn’t as surprised as
he felt when the Johnnie suddenly stabbed her to
the heart. Directly that happened, he found him-
self back in his own room still brushing his bald
head for dinner, and when he had finished dress-
ing and went down, the house was just as he had
left it, and no sign of either Johnnie or girl.
Content to treat the whole thing as a halluci-
nation due to something or other, he said nothing
to his host (it isn’t etiqaette to allude to visions of
violence in baronial halls—it’s as bad as freely
talking of tambourines in a certain society family
of Sydney), and the affair somewhat slipped his
memory—but imagine his horror, when adjourning
W1 j ( , anc L ther stranger, to meet, in flesh
and blood, the Johnnie and the maiden whom he
had seen in his alleged remarkable vision. They
were engaged, too, and apparently much attached to
one another. At first Mr. Asterisk (as I call him
though his name’s well-knoWn in Australia—but
men don t like to have the reputation of^beinir
dreamers of dreams unless they include the name
of the Oup-winner) thought to warn the lady or
advise the youth, but on second thoughts decided
to wait. He has returned to Australia and is
still waiting for the tragic denouement so unex-
pectedly revealed to him —a denouement for which
he can’t help living in hope, I’ll bet.
Thomas Walker’s alleged comedy-drama, “The
Hypnotist,’’ was last week put up for one horrible
night before a rather weird audience at Sydney
Standard Theatre—the happy hunting-ground
of amateurdom, by the way. The drama—in five
acts and fifteen scenes—did not hypnotise one to
any great extent. Everybody had a grievance
which required a great deal of explanation, and
one girl, with an awe-inspiring coiffure and a rigid
backbone, took her sorrows in such large mouth-
fuls that she suggested a hurried traveller trying
to eat a railway sandwich in five seconds. The
leading lady, on the other hand (she was billed as
large as the author), told her long and sinful story
in fox-terrier snaps. The maid-servant, Juliet, (in
real life, when a hireling turns up with a senti-
mental name like that her mistress promptly tells
her she always calls the housemaid Bridget) was
the most reasonable performer of them all.
Most of the men seemed to be trying to find their
way out (all except the author, who absolutely
revelled in the middle of the stage), and even the
hero s fearful and wonderful dressing - jacket
couldn’t entirely blind you to the fact that he
wasn’t a jeune premier every night of his life,
though, mind you, nobody short of Bernhardt
could have sent any of Mr. Walker’s sorry plati-
tudes home. _ How a man who can write such
dramatic fustian must envy the author of “ Blow
for Blow !”
The amateur actress is a difficult creature to
handle, and the higher she is in the social world
the harder she is to manage. A gifted lady, who
translates snappy French plays into adequate
English, has a really charming comedy on the
stocks which she wanted to get a good amateur
crowd to play. And she had them all readied up,
too—but when it transpired that one of the
characters was the hero’s mistress, the leading-
lady-to-be froze right up to her toupe, and re-
marked in measured tones, “ Certainly we know,
madaine, that such things unfortunately exist;
but, all the same, we cannot, of course, appear in
a play with such a character among the dramatis
personce. How lovely to know that Society—
where most of the spiciest on dits come from —
really has a vague idea that “such things do
exist! ”
There is no question that - such things do exist.
A yarn goes the rounds re a well-advertised
Southern politician that his dove-cotes are
dotted here, there, and everywhere. At a
parliamentary ceremony held not long ago
it is said that all these nymphs assembled
to witness the public; gyrations of their adorer,
though, of course, the gold-washed lady in blue
and pink stripes didn’t know the brunette in tan-
boots, or the red-haired girl with too much cos-
metic on her freckles. One of these ladies tore off
to the festival in such a hurry, and in such new
clothes, that the price-tags were still pinned on
her garments.
I am rather surprised to hear of the squabble
between Joseph Rignold and Rev. Geo. Walters
as to who is the true author of the dramatisation
of Moses best after-dinner story—“ Joseph of
Canaan. ’ One could more easily imagine Mr.
Walters boasting that he had most to do with the
magnificent staging, or in designing Maude
irotiphar Williamson’s pink temptation-garment,
than tnat Rignold should try to insinuate that he
had a hand in writing the pointless modern talkee-
talkee, which always struck me as being a
withered kernel in a fine, glossy nut. “Joseph of
Canaan ’ is well worth seeing, on account of the
extreme efficiency of his coat of paint, but you
feel that you want to get away from his misfit
language now and then.
Apropos of George Rignold, the extreme virtue
of his parting speech, thanking his friends and
(non) supporters for the awful handicap of a silver
tea-and-coffee set to people bent on travelling,
and summing up his career at Her Majesty’s, was
rather funny, especially to people who had had
the pleasure of hearing the prudish George (he
who scorned to stage frisky but paying plays 1)
unburden his soaring soul on a certain festive
occasion concerning the nakedness of his chorus-
girls.
Mam zelle Nitouche,” at Sydney Lyceum
last Saturday, was double-banked by an “Arca-
dian Eve, a bright and bubblesome trifle in which
Wallace Brownlow and Florence Young, most
artistically draped, appeared to great advantage.
In Mam zelle Nitouche,” Juliet Wray's Denise
was ala Nellie Stewart; it reminded me of a
shadow of Nellie (shadows often lengthen one’s
nose a bit, you know, and make other physical
alterations) which she had left behind, or for-
gotten to pack, or something ; for all Nellie’s biz
was served up again without sauce piquante. It
was certainly a smart copy for so recent an ama-
teur to give ; still, a touch of originality here and
there would have looked promising and been
gratefully received in front. In the “Fair
Indian ” scene, for example, Miss Wray wore a
different, more discreet costume than Nellie’s
Nautch draperies (which was possibly the reason
why Wallace Brownlow, whose Fernand was as
convincingly military and melodious as ever, left
the stage while she was raspily singing a waltz-
song, whereas he always sat around in Nellie’s
era), and she would have perpetrated a novelty
had she browned her face and arms and changed
her hair, instead of coming out h la Nellie to play
a freak Indian with golden locks and a pink-and-
white complexion.
Mark Hambourg’s Sydney season, with J. E.
Sykes for his guide, philosopher, and friend, pro-
mises to boom, and will be divided ’twixt the
Town and Centenary halls, provided the little
genius isn’t; consumed by his own sacred fire
meanwhile. Mark is a brilliant pianist, and yet
most women over 30 feel they’d like to be a
mother or a grandmother to him, just to coddle
him up. This spirit was so strong in one kind old
lady that, as the co. got into the carriage to go to
railway-station, after its last concert in.
Melbourne lown Hall, this motherly person
pushed forward and handed the second Ruben-
stein a bottle of liniment. “ I’ve heard yon suffer
from rheumatism,” she said, “so I thought I’d
bring you this bottle of stuff, for it’s a certain
relief. You’ll find full directions inside. Good-
bye.” Possibly the only time this incident has
been duplicated was when a certain well-known
singer, whose complexion is rather far-fetched in
the matter of pimples, was respectfully handed a
box ot pills by a practical admirer. Mark H. has
many admirers, by the way—the most original
being two young ladies, who wrote to him jointly,
asking for a photograph apiece. “If you cannot
send us one each,” they wrote, “one between us
will do. We 11 draw lots for it, or else do the
Solomon trick and cut it in two.”

Every woman is at heart ” —a missionary, and
when they massacre her in large quantities in
China, or have her on toast in the Cannibal
Island, she breaks out in a safer place next time
but break out she must or perish. Two English
virgins, at present visiting Australia, have come
to encourage the solid formation, with a flag fly-
ing on the top, of the Police Christian Institute—
for christianising the foorce is the newest idea
now that the Chinese have begun retaliating with
battle-axes. In the working-out the idea is much
more amusing and safer and more thrilling than
trying to wean a slant-eyed Chow from worship-
ping his ancestors. Policemen are frequently
handsomer especially the mounted variety, who
are alleged to drop into their skin-fit riding
trousers from a great height, as they cannot pos-
sibly get into them any other way. There used
to be an officer (on foot) at the corner of King and
George streets, who. in the opinion of the fair sex
of Sydney, only needed fewer clothes, a wash of
pipeclay and a pedestal, to be the Apollo Belvidere,’
He arrested the women wholesale—optically, at
least—but lam grieved to hear that the effect of
Sydney beauty was too much for him in the end,
for he committed suicide one morning. If police
missions had started in his time, what a rush
there’d have been to convert the King-st. Adonis!
I he Police Christian Institute is comprehensive,'
in its English, at least, for I read that “a pro-
vincial orphanage” has been established. But
how fortunate it hasn’t been hastily christened
9 unc^’n ß’ Asylum ? ” Even the known virtue
of the I orce and the pious fervor of the mis-
sioneress wouldn’t quite overcome the prejudice
there is against that style of establishment ? But
I really don’t see that the police want converting. •
People accredited with possessing a watch and ;i
terrace of houses apiece are generally pillars of the
church already.
It is a suspicious circumstance that most of the
women who pursue the soul of Policeman X
should be single—it is this singleness, by the way,
which is responsible for the crass ignorance dis-
played by those females who go evangelising
round the gaols. For instance, evangelists used
to go round to the various watch-houses on Sun-
day mornings with pails full of washy tea with the
object of slaking the burning thirst of the Satur-
day crop of inebriates to whom, at that juncture,
it would have been a real kindness to give a hair
of the dog that bit them. Heaps of damp bread-
and-butter were also laid on without mercy—the
result, I suppose, of so many bread-and-butter
misses having a hand in it. Though it may, after
all, have been due to the garden ignorance of the
average pious female who preserves her native
rawness whether she’s buried three husbands or
has never had even the ghost of one.
“ Why does my friend, Brown,” writes a man
to me, “appear to be dressed like a gentleman,
and his wife always like an upper servant of poor
taste? Why does Brown always look even in an
old suit as if he had been ordered for the suit, and
Mrs. Brown look as if her dress had been ordered
for someone else? Why does Mrs. Brown always
have a lot of little bits of things about her body
that don’t seem to belong either to her or to her
dress? These things have always puzzled me. I
wish Mrs, Brown could look more at home in her
clothes ; also I wish that she would not change all
the funny little things about her dress for another
set of funny little things just as I have got used
to the old set.
“ 1 was beginning to think that I knew the use
of these big bows at the back of her neck when,
behold ! they have disappeared ; and now I don’t
know how she is to avoid that rheumatic nain
without the bow. She has now got some sort of
white muslin thing round her neck, and I have
not yet been able to settle what it is for. Oh,
I think she must be cutting her wisdom-teeth and
she is perhaps expecting to dribble, I do wish
some powerful statesman could regulate these
little things for Mrs. Brown, she is so very fluc-
tuating. Two years ago she wore quiet colors. I
was sometimes not quite sure when I saw her
coming round the corner whether it was not
Brown himself in the old grey suit; but this year
she has patches of bright colors in the most un-
expected places, and I wonder whether it is a very
large parrot that has been let out of a case by
accident.
“ I remember long ago I used to wonder how
she got out of her little tight sleeves ; and I can’t
help wondering how I am to get in at the same
door with these sleeves she is wearing' now.
Yesterday, when I met Mr. and Mrs. Brown at
the show there was a bit of a crush, and I got a
blow from some hard substance just on my funny-
bone. On looking round, the nearest thing was
that big sleeve, and it looked so soft and billowy I
•could not suspect it. I wonder if it has a hoop
below its undulating surface. It is not very long
ago that she had a hat with a crown no bigger
than an egg-cup ; and now her hat has a big soft
sort of crown that might cover a fair-sized brain.
1 wonder if her brain has grown recently. Oh,
i §'• U P’ have a sort of idea that if
she did not change the fashion of her clothes quite
so often maybe her architecture would be more
easy to master ; and, also, her clothes might in
time come to sit with some grace on her, as her
husband’s coat does on him.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
ihe managers of a Toorak church, which for
years topped the ‘Hospital Sunday ’ score, com-
plain bitterly of failing finances. Slowly but
surely, their staunchest supporters relinquish
their pews and depart to cheaper localities, hand-
ing over their ancestral mansions for protracted
terms to tenants, generally of Semitic origin—
most undesirable residents from the Kirk point of
view.”
ITours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

In re Crick’s allegation that Judge Win-
deyer and Salomons, Q.C., had made it up and
were now great friends, Salomons thus spoke in
N.S.W. Council last week :
Everyone knows that, in my judgment, I received a
great wrong and a great injustice from Mr. Justice Win-
deyer some eight or nine years ago. In the language of
Mr. Crick and Mr. Meagher, his action forced me, in the
interests of the colony to which I was so indebted, and
having regard to the administration of justice, which was
more important than my private wishes, to set aside and
forego the object which, as regards professional success, I
had had before my eyes for years. Let no one he oblivious
of this : that I was appointed Chief-Justice, and had the
Queen’s Patent. (Cheers.) But I took the view, although
I admit with great sorrow, perhaps as a punishment to
me, that I must deny myself the sitting in the chief seat
of justice because, did I sit there, there would be
scenes in the Court that would destroy the confidence of
the people in the administration of justice. Then
afterwards, in a moment of passion, that same
judge not very long ago dealt out to me a measure
of language in the Court which wounded me more
deeply than any words of mine can convey. But
I do not care to have a life-long feud with any man. 1
am too conscious of the uncertainty of human life and my
own failings, and my hope of winning pardon for my own
sins, to deny to him the forgiveness I hope to receive for
my own weaknesses. I became reconciled. I admit that
before that matter we had been long and deep friends;
but never since that day have I eaten or drunk a drop in
the house of Mr. Justice Windeyer, although we are on
friendly and amicable terms, as is necessary for the ad-
ministration of justice, seeing my position in the Court
and his position on the Bench.
Here everybody recalled the words of Shylock
I will talk with you, walk with you,
But I will not eat with you, drink with you, nor pray
with you.

A DESPICABLE NUISANCE.
(to the editor op The Bulletin.)
Sir, —I am a regular subscriber to your paper,
and read it attentively, and consider that I have
a perfect right to protest against the impudence
of advertisers, who, starting with a matter of
public interest, spring upon the unsuspecting
reader a recommendation of their wares. Why
should the übiquitous advertisement be intruded
upon the privacy of my reading by jugglery only
worthy of a thimble-rigging mountebank ? Are
readers to be for ever harassed by these pachyder-
matous purloiners of our time and attention ? The
limit to my endurance was reached last night.
Whilst following up the Premier’s remarks to the
electors I was faced with the statement that
Frossard’s Cavour Cigars are mild and fragrant.
Who shall deliver us? Everyone already knows
this ; also, that the nicotine is extracted in vacuo,
and that they are obtainable everywhere, I have
smoked no other for years.—l am, &c..

With the arrival of Lord Brassey and suite
there is to be an unprecedented bicycle boom.
The question of a becoming rational costume has
hitherto been a stumbling-block to the popularity
of bicycling among the fair sex, but apparently
demand has now created a supply and the diffi-
culty is solved. Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson & Sons,
of Collins-street., Melb., exhibit the latest styles
of costumes, such as are worn in the Bois de
Boulogne in Paris, where every pretty woman is
a wheelist in the present year of grace.*

The 13 married pressmen on the two
Melb. dailies possess only half-a-dozen children
among the lot. The poor scribe has little time
for such matters. In this respect, journalism
seems a trifle worse—or better—than bicycling.
An utter dislike for exercise and public
show is one of Mark Twain’s peculiarities. If he
can possibly help it he won’t move out of his hotel.
This is a boon to the “ Much-Travelled,” for M.T.
is essentially a “ show ” star. If people could in-
spect Mark’s shaggy mane gratis on the Block
daily a good many would be prepared to take his
evening humor for granted.

The full name of the new Governor of
Queensland is Charles Wallace Alexander Napier
Cochrane Baillie, Baron Lamington. His initials
have already been packed and despatched to
Brisbane per cargo steamer, and the nobleman
himself follows at an early date.

of novelist Dickens, isnow, itis alleged, anappraiser

probably made for the same reason that E.B.L.
was elected to Parliament —viz., because “he took
the trouble to be born.”
A recently-made Vic. knight was in atten-
dance at a certain rural function recently, when
he was approached by one of a knot of bucolic
admirers, with, “Good afternoon to you. Mister
Blank!” “Hush!” exclaimed a consternated
onlooker. “You mustn’t call him mister—he’s a
knight, man.” “Oh, he is, is he !” was the dis-
gusted reply. “ Then / don’t shake hands with
’im.”
“Shot a man wanst” is a Melbourneism,
which appears in type again over the Akehurst
business, and is not exactly understandable by
outsiders. O’Hea was a M.P., one of Syme’s
hacks. He travelled to Colac with two strangers
who were discussing politics. O’H. interposed
with “ Sorr, I shot a man wanst for saying less
than you are saying now.” He never heard the
last of it.
The late J. M. Toohey was a South Mel-
bournite by birth and always had some sort of in-
terest in that busted suburb. When Mat. Lar-
kin’s building society “shortages” were first dis-
covered, and thought to be a mere trifle of £lO,OOO
or so, Toohey came over from Sydney, it was
said with a view to pulling Matthias through.
But he found that a rescue of “ honest Matt.”
was out of the question.
George Coppin, after bemoaning the fate
of his bank-shares and other investments, said to
a Melb. newspaper reporter : “It is a sad ending
to an eventful life, is it not?” But the end of a
successful man’s life isn’t solely a matter of L.S.D.
On Coppin’s theory the careers of some of the
world’s brightest men were sad from start to finish,
inasmuch as the poor devils never had any capital
worth mentioning.

The Saturday Review, noticing a slovenly
compilation by that ridiculous person, Albert
Calvert (most shameless and pretentious of shoddy
self-advertisers), on “The Exploration of Aus-
tralia,” objects that he has omitted all mention of
the work in this direction of Burke and Wills,
“ who are so famous iu the history of Australia as
to have given their names to streets.” The
Saturday reviewer evidently thinks that Bourke-
street, Melb., is called after explorer Burke, in-
stead of after Gov. Bourke.
A London paper speaks of Lady Hope-
tonn’s “ Rubenesque style of beauty.” When in
Melbourne Lady Hopetoun was tall and slim, and
her features were refined, and her face oval ;
whereas Rubens invariably painted big, fat,
coarse Dutch wenches, with Christmas-pudding
faces, and busts like mountains. Even in his
famous “Rape of Sabine Women” he made the
ladies like washerwomen for coarse bulkiness. Is
Lady Hopetoun “ fillin’ out? ” \

with the trio in Melbourne says : “I was very
much amused by the conversation of the Tramp
Abroad. His quaint turns of expression and his
sparks of wit kept me in a continual titter of mer-
riment. But I noticed that the Mark-ess and
Miss Mark took no live interest in papa’s talk.
Madame lolled in her chair and put on a far-away
look, whilst Miss read ‘ Love in Idleness ’ —a
shilling’s-worth of fiction in blue covers. Perhaps

When Mark Hambourg was executing one
of his most thrilling pieces at Melb. Town Hall,
the head of probably the biggest firm of Australian
stenographers watched him throughout with a rapt
expression, his hands grasping the seat in front of
him, and his eyes starting from his face. As
Mark struck the final chord of about 24 notes the
man of transcription leaned back and took a long
breath. “Great Scott,” he said, “ what a type-
writer he’d make ! ”
In a lecture last week on “Prominent
Australians” Angus Cameron, M.P. (N.S.W.),
said that “ Like many others, ‘Old Jack ’ Robert-
son left public life poorer than he found it, and
the <£lo,ooo voted by Parliament to him upon his
retirement was spent in paying his debts—a
matter that was not generally known, but one
with which people ought to he acquainted.” Mr.
Cameron might have emphasised his point by
stating that some of the politicians who helped
the vote through Parliament were themselves
large creditors of “old Jack.”
One good point about ex-Premier Dibbs is
that, since his rejection at Tamworth, he has
never been known to wail about his defeat, or to
say that he didn’t want “ hoffis,” or to complain
about the ingratitude of his country, or to lament
in any shape or form about his downfall. He
hasn’t even remarked, as Shiels did, that he would
take his defeat in a cheerful spirit; he has simply
taken it, and said nothing about it. And he
hasn’t once remarked to a reporter that he was
glad to he out of politics. All he did after his
fall was to go home and hitch the horse to Ins
famous personally-invented water-raising machine,
and when the horse proved unequal to the job
instead of lamenting about the failure he bar-
nessed the family cow along with it, and when the
horse and cow together proved that the machine
was no good the old man took the collapse of his
invention in a cordial spirit and sent to Sydney
for an oil engine. Nobody ever met adversity in
a happier frame of mind than old, blundering,

Reay, editor of Melb. Herald, IS a whole
heap of things. In addition to being a journalist
of abnormal activity, with an insatiable appetite
for work, he is captain of a co. of mounted rifles,
also secretary and treasurer to same; he is like-
wise a temperance-lecturer, a mason, a female
franchise - leaguer, an oddfellow, an Australian
native, a federation-leaguer, a social democrat, a
Yarra-bank orator, an anti-sweater, a protec-
t.omst, a capital-punishment abolitionist, a cur-
rency crank ; is treasurer of the Institute of Jour-
nalists, vice-president of the Victorian Shorthand
Writers Association, and a candidate for Parha-
merit, with the ambition to become Minister of
Defence, and reform that rqttenest of Vic. Govt.
departments. Though he is constantly taking
part in some public movement, delivering lectures,
and reading papers, he never puts Ins own name
in his paper, and seldom allows anybody else to
do it. He began as a sailor, turned schoolmaster,
had a turn at sugar-refining, bought two news-
papers, 200 miles apart, and ran both of them,
being his own editor, printer, and publisher. He
is first into the Hkrai.D office in the morning and
is last to leave at night. Some say he never
sleeps. He is 36 years old.

This in allusion to the prayer-for-rain fake, and

parent devices to catch the “ pious” vote. Bub il
Jacob Garrard is wise he will cease canting in
public. Every Government, however holy, has a
woman or two in its cupboard, and when those
women come out nobody talks about their God-
fearing proprietors.
Zimmerman, the world’s champion cyclist,
is a six-footer, and impresses one as being more a
lightning sprinter than a cycling scorcher. Ha
has a very youthful appearance, ant), like the
famous Tom Busst in his palmy wheeling days,
has ideal “cycling legs,” all bone and sinew.
“ Zim.” says he doesn’t particularly want to race,
but will do so at any meeting on receipt of .£lOO
to start. His chief game is to boom the “Nim-
rod ’’ cycles, holding as he does a large interest in
the firm.

ATTACK ON A TAMWORTH LADY.
HOW IT OCCURRED —HER DESCRIPTION OF THE CIR-
CUMSTANCES, AND THE OVERWHELMING TES-
TIMONY OF PROMINENT WITNESSES.
Mrs. Matheson’s statement of this ferociom
attack is so extiaordinary that some readers might
think it incredible, or that she was deceived by
appearances, but her lamentable condition was
admitted by her local doctor, and corroborated by
the physician in charge at the Electric Baths in
Sydney, and her sufferings were witnessed by all
of her immediate friends and neighbours. Con-
sequently it is not to be wondered at that our in-
terviewer left her thoroughly convinced of the
truth of her statements, which should be placed
before the public. In fact, what she suffered and
endured —her narrow escape from death—is the
talk of the town, and her home has been besieged
by friends offering congratulations. Her own
statement, made July 16th, 1894 : “ It is now five
years since I had occasion to use any kind of
medicine, having been cured at that time of a
very long and severe illness by taking Warner’s
Safe Remedies. The complaint I suffered from
was muscular rheumatism ; my neck, arms, legs,
and back were much swollen and stiff. I could
not turn my head or lift my arms, neither could I
move myself into a sitting posture. The kidneys
were also in a very bad state, the pains in the
back most excruciating, appetite feeble, and my
condition altogether helpless. My friends did not
expect that 1 could possibly live. I had spent
many pounds in advice and prescriptions, all to
no benefit, and also underwent a month’s treat-
ment at the E'ectrio Baths in Sydney, but re-
turned worse than ever, and without any hope of
getting about again. At this juncture a medical
pamphlet containing the account of a remarkable
cure by Warner’s Safe Remedies in the case of a
gentleman whose symptoms were similar to my
own was given me. I resolved at once to com-
mence with a course of Safe Cure and Safe Rheu-
matic Cure alternately, and was delighted to find
some relief from the first two bottles. Persever-
ing until I had taken seven bottles, and one bottle
of Safe Pills, I became free from all aches and
pains, and able to eat and sleep well and help my-
self without any assistance whatever. To this
day I have had no return of the complaint.
Many friends and neighbours are ready to vouch
for tiie truth of this statement, for they say they
never expected me to be about among them again,
and were greatly surprised that any medicine
could do what the Safe Remedies have done for
me. I have lived in the colonies forty-five years,
and raised a family of nineteen children, and am
well known throughout the district of Tamwqrth,
where my unexpected recovery has caused quite a
sensation. .
