POLITICAL POINTS
.
Journalist; “After long observation ot
the Vic. Parliamentary refreshment-room, I con-
tend it ought to he abolished, and that a neighbor-
ing caterer would, spring up, in the natural order
of things.”
Of the total purchase-money received by
Q. Govt, for land sold since Separation, about
6£ millions, not £1 has gone for railway con-
struction or redemption of public debt. Consoli-
dated Revenue has swallowed up the whole—for
payment of interest.
Since the Townsville-Hughenden line was
built it has earned a net profit over all charges of
more than A 270 000. Now that the Winton exten-
sion is to be added (estimated to cost only a little
over .£2OOO per mile for the first 45 miles) it will
be still more lucrative.

The Q. railway figures for the last 14 years
£lB9 705; Southern and Western system owes
the country £2 153 861. No wonder the Queen-
street politician objects to Separation.

The late JOHN ROPER, the last survivor but
one of Leichhardt's. expedition.
Q. Government omits nothing to secure
election-success. It alternately threatens, whee-
dles, bribes. The latest move is to dangle before
Parliament railways from Charieville to Cunna-
mulla, 121 miles ; Hughenden to Winton, 65 miles;
South and North Rockhampton, 2| miles ; branch
to Broadmount, 15£ miles.
Melb. people have again asserted them-
selves democratically at an anti-sweating meet-
ing by refusing to accept a motion of thanks to the
Mayor and City Council for the use of the Town
Hall. The Mayor and Council are not entitled to
any thanks for allowing the people to use that
which belongs to the people.
Among the exhibits confidentially sub-
mitted by Lormer to the Melb. Crown Law
authorities in substantiation of his denunciation
of the honorarium magistracy were some strange
Chinese documents, purporting to be receipts for
cash paid for squaring beaks in lottery-cases. The
valiant Lormer evidently means business.
Opposition-leader John Downer (S.A.), on
different occasions and with much frequency,
brings two charges against the Kingston Govt.
(1) That its policy is unstatesmanlike and alto-
gether inadequate to the needs of the country, and
(2) That its policy in all essential particulars
was stolen from his Govt., which was defeated in
June, 1893.
Wood, M.P. (S.A.), moved in the Assem-
bly, during the discussion of the Public Salaries
Reduction Bill, that no M.P. while holding his
seat should be allowed to engage in any other
trade, calling, or profession. “My political duties
take up all my time,” he said, “ and we don’t
allow other public servants to earn money by
private work.”
A Sydney speculator proposes to run a
railway from Queensland to the Westralian coast,
to carry trains running 150 miles an hour, with a
canal alongside to take heavy merchandise. Grape-
vines are to be trellised along the line, which is to
be further rendered valuable by the provision of
telegraph and telephone communication. All now
wanted is money.
In N.S. Wales, 97| P er cent, of the tobacco-
growers are Chinese, 90 per cent, of scrub-cutters,
75 per cent, of market-gardeners, 74 per cent, of
vegetable hawkers, 57 per cent, of tin-miners, 50
per cent, of ring barkers, 47 per cent, of green-
grocers, 48 per cent, of hotel cooks, &c., &c.
When the Chow once lays hold of an industry,
you can bet that he is going to monopolise
that industry, if there are enough of him in the
country to do it.
N.S.W. Government, if it is wise or in
earnest about Constitutional reform, will pass
a complete Referendum Bill through the Assem-
bly. before it sends to the Council a second
Reform Bill, containing referendum clauses for
which there is no political machinery. Try the
Councillors with pure jam before giving them
jam with rhubarb in it.
According to Vic. Opposition, Premier
Turner is at present wandering around the House
holding in his hand an empty skull (his own), and
begging that somebody will, for the love of God,
drop an idea into it. A pretty robust metaphor.
He doesn’t trouble Patterson’s side of the House,
however. To go there in search of ideas would be
like going to a goat’s house to look for wool.
“ Dick ” Sleath, M.P., was stonewalling
the Goulburn-Crookwell Railway Bill in N.S.W.
Assembly, and picturing the affected district as a
howling waste. A voice : “ Were you ever there?”
Sleath : “ I resided in that district for a consider-
able time.” Samson Haynes; “Your oppor-
tunities for observation were very limited.” Then
the House remembered that Sleath had resided in
Goulburn gaol!
According to Premier Nelson, no one can
hire a subject of Japan to come to Queensland
without depositing a substantial sum with the
Jap. Govt. Nelson knows perfectly well that
Australia fears the free Japanese immigrant more
than the coolie laborer. According to the same
authority, the Japanese women on Thursday
Island have been smuggled thither disguised as
men. The Jap. Govt., when informed of the
incident, took immediate steps to prevent a recur-
rence.
Thus Brisbane Courier : “We are trying to
prove that Europeans can live and thrive in field-
work upon which the flood of tropical heat is
poured down during a tropical summer, and re-
markable success has been achieved. Along the
coast-lands of Queensland white farmers are
founding homes, and Queensland has the only
white agricultural settlement in tropical Aus-
tralia.” And only five years ago the white man
couldn’t work there at all—according to the same
authority.
N.S. W. Education Minister Garrard grows
enthusiastic over the W.C.T.U. idea of instruct-
ing public-school children in the evils of drink by
means of elementary text-books. But care must
be taken to make the lessons physiologically cor-
rect. Out of the 50 States of the American Union
44 have passed measures making such instruction
obligatory, but in many cases it has been found
that the information imparted consisted merely of
the yells, whoops, and upside-down statistics of
wild-eyed women.
Grifiihvraith’s prophecy that the re-intro-
dnotion of Kanakas into Leperland would give
new life to the sugar-industry and provide mar-
kets for maize and horses at big prices, has proved
a will-o’-the-wisp. Maize and horses have never
been lower in prices ; nor, indeed, has sugar itself.
In the biggest Q. mill and refinery—Millaquin,
Bundaberg—there has recently been a fid. cut in
the 6s. a day wages, with the bosses reduced Is. a
day, and one scientific employe 2s. fid. a day. It
is becoming clearer every day that the Kanaka is
not white industry’s feeder, but its blood-sucker.

raise from <£500,000 to

Speaking of the “ move on ” street-regula-
tion, Sydney D.T. scornfully refers to “loungers,
whose place is in the parks, if it is anywhere.”
That is true Tory contempt for the unemployed :
his place is nowhere. Yet the labor-hind borrows
his politics from, and votes at the bidding of, the
D.T. ; and Labor M’sP. turn public somersaults
in order to grovel for its favors. Yes; their place
is nowhere, “if it is anywhere.”
Once more the S.M. Herald girds at
Maorilaud and Victoria for not establishing a
policy of reciprocity with the Fieetrade Govt, of
N.S. Wales. What is there to be reciprocated?
Premier Reid is, or says he is, going to make
Sydney and other N.S. W. ports absolutely free
ports—not to Victoria, nor to Maorilaud, nor to
reciprocating countries only, but to all the world.
This is to be done for Premier Reid’s own pur-
poses. Then how can he talk “ reciprocity ” and
“free ports to all-comers” in one and the same
breath? He is going to saw off the reciprocity
bough on which he is sitting—if he means what
he says.
According to Coghlan, in New South Wales
rather more than per cent, of the agricultural
and pastoral freehold land is occupied by people
who pay rent to the owners ; in South Australia,
in 1891, the last year for which statistics were
available, 66 per cent, is rented land ; in Maori-
land 36 per cent. No returns are supplied by the
other provinces, but evidently Australia is getting
on well towards the formation of a squirearchy on
the fine old Hinglish plan, accompanied by fire-
works and rejoicings and old ale among the
loyal tenantry when the heir comes of age,
and diversified by rheumatism caused by lying
behind a damp hedge at night with a gun looking
for the agent.
Premier Reid's attitude is practically this :
“ Unless you pass my Loan Bill I won’t help the
unemployed.’’ Consequently, the Loan Bill
passes, because whoever opposes it is set down as
a heartless ruffian with no sympathy for the poor.
Some day another Premier may say: “Unless
you let me build land-grant railways, or unless
you let me sell the State railways to my shindy-
kit, I won’t find any work for the unemployed,”
which will be equally logical. The present N.S. W.
Govt, won’t find work for the unemployed unless
the country first allows it to cook the public
accounts and produce a sham surplus by charging
current expenses to loan moneys. The alleged
friend of the poor looks tolerably mean under
these conditions.

A STAGE MARRIAGE.
JOHNNIR: “ Is that charming Miss De Tartington MARRIED?"
BUSKIS (of the [?]healre Royal): “Can’t say; not to her present
husband, in any case.”
According to Mr. Nakagawa, Japanese
consul in Hongkong, the Japs are failures as
colonists. Accustomed to a simple life at home,
they are unprepared and unfitted for the fierce
competition they must encounter: abroad. In
Hawaii alone have they been successful, while in
Fiji and New Caledonia they have proved
disastrous failures; also (Mr. Nakagawa asserts)
in Queensland.
In this issue of The Bulletin appears the
portrait of good - looking Alex. Montgomery,
whose stories and sketches, printed in this paper
during the past three years, have made his signa-
ture grateful to the eyes of every appreciative
reader. Mr. Montgomery is Scottish, owns to
nearly 50 years of age, and looks about two-thirda
of them. The Bulletin regards his work,
particularly his Bornean stories, as some of
the most vigorous and valuable yarn-stuff that
has ever appeared in its columns. Mr. Mont-
gomery’s literary style is a model strong,
clear, brilliant, and picturesque—lacking only
a little more sympathy and variety to be
perfect. His matter is rare and original,
abounding in interest, and salted with sardonic
humor. When a volume of Mr. Montgomery’*
collected stories appears, which will probably be
soon, The Bulletin predicts for it a warm wel-
come and an instant success.
John Roper, who died at Merriwa, N.S.
Wales, on Sept. 15 last, was one of Australia’s
historic explorers. Born in Gayton-Thorpe,
county Norfolk, Eng., in 1824, he reached this
country in ’43. He was thus only 20 years old
when he embarked his small savings in the famous
expedition which Leichhardt led from Moreton
Bav to Port Essington in ’44. Besides Leichhardt
and Roper, the adventurous party originally in-
cluded James Calvert (no relation of the absurd
Cockney tourist who is now puffing himself in
England as an “explorer”), John Murphy, Pember-
ton Hodgson, naturalist Gilbert, Win. Phillips(con-
vict), an American negro named Caleb, and two
aboriginals, Charley and Harry. Hodgson and
Caleb returned to Brisbane before going far. The
others continued their journey and reached Port
Essington, on the Northern coast, on Dec. 17, 1845,
a year and four months after starting. Thence they
returned in the schooner Heroine to Sydney,
which was reached March 29, 1846, after all hope
of them had been given up. Roper proved him-
self a most intrepid and valuable member of the
party. On 25th June, 1845, during an attack by
blacks near the head of the Gulf of Carpentaria,
Gilbert was killed and Roper and Calvert were
severely wounded. On 13th Oct., ’45, Roper dis-
covered the river which bears his name. The
party was in extremity for want of water, and
Roper rode on a little distance, ascended a
low hill, and saw stretched before him a
magnificent country through which a fine river
slowly meandered to the Gulf. When the party
reached Sydney, a private and public subscription
was raised, totalling .£1000; of which Leichhardt
received .£6OO and Roper and Calvert <£l2s each.
The next 20 years of Roper’s life were spent chiefly
in pastoral pursuits, and in ’6B he was appointed
a Govt, inspector of stock at Merriwa —an office
which he held until his retirement on a small
pension, two years ago. Roper made a genial,
hearty, vigorous old man, performed his duties
admirably, and gained the goodwill of everybody
he had to do with. His death, at 71, robs Aus-
tralia of one of the few survivors of the grand old
pioneering band.
A MISSION-HYMN.
“ We are doing- a glorious work in India and China."—
{Sermon recently given in Brisbane on the Chinese
massacres.)
“ Grog-shops exist and flourish in nearly all the large
towns of India." — (Report of Wesleyan Missionary Con-
ference.)
Behold on burning India’s coral strand
The swart Hindoo arise, baptised and bland ;
Behold him change, for fashions fair and new,
His reptile gods and crocodilean crew !
Forsaking Ganges’ grateful holy stream
He seeks the fount where Bass’s glories gleam,
Deserts the Sacred Ox he loved of old
And bows before our Sacred Calf of Gold.
From Juggernaut he flies to Jug-or-Naught,
And flouts the faith inane his father taught;
Lo! Buddh and Brahm he scouts with scornful
jeer,
And cries, “O Bible great and greater Beer 1 ”
Long, long, O Brahmin-ridden Bengalee,
May scoffers mark thee crook a Christian knee
While pious Bull, exulting, tells the tale :
“ He reads our Scriptures and he drinks our ale! ’>
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

Dear Bulletin, — lie the Nunneley analytic
blunder (your par. of 28 Sept.)-a man in Eng-
land was once actually under sentence of death,
before laylor, the noted toxicologist, discovered
that the arsenic which he had previously testified
to finding in the fluids of the supposed murdered
person, had been accidentally introduced by him-
self during the process of analysis. Isn’t it mere
than likely—-since the tube-test for arsenic will
show the presence of the 500,000 th of a grain
that an unsuspected trace of the drug in some of
the re-agents employed has in this way sent many
a man to the gallows ? Speculum.
For coughs, colds you can’t beat Colemane
and Sons Eucalypte Extract.*
Sydney’s militant cleric, Bev. Jas. Clarke,
who was, rightly or wrongly, arrested and fined
tor drunkenness, the other day, has had his
license to preach cancelled by the Primate. The
police department hates Clarke like poison ever
since he got atheist Walker out of its clutches;
the Supreme Court bench wishes him at Tirnbuo-
too; and 0. of E. ecclesiastics moan in agony
over the Man Who Won’t Keep Quiet. Clarke
says that the Primate’s inhibition is illegal, and
declines to surrender his church, and the Primate
hesitates to turn him out by force for fear of the
scandal. The Rev. Jas. has now three appeals
under weigh—-one to Queen Vic., against Judge
Wmdeyer’s divorce law ; one against his convic-
tion for drunkenness; and one against his Primate.
Altogether, a very pretty pickle. And there is a
good deal ofi y -npathy with Clarke among those
who know the facts.

It is reckoned that in tlie recent severe
weather 40,000 to 60,000 newly - shorn sheep
perished one night in the Millie (N.S.W.) district.
In one case, out of 1080 sheep shorn 1000 perished
durin? the ensuing night. Another station lost
fully 4000.
A record In fecundity. A mare belonging
to W. R. Yirgoe, of Mathonra Station (N.S.W.)
was covered by a half-bred Welsh pony on 3rd
and 12th Sept, only, and was afterwards kept in a
paddock with mares only, the only stallions within
15 miles round being always kept in stables
securely. On 12th Aug. following she dropped a
filly foal ; eight weeks later —viz., on Gth Oct.—
she dropped a colt foal, and 17 days later on yet a
third foal (colt). She suckled all three, and was
a splendid mother, not allowing the other mares
near her triple brood.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
ihere being only one Austral wheel-race a year
with its first prize of £2OO, and second of £4O, makes
every competitor a trier. Therefore, the bookmakers will
lay the odds at an average price of 16 to 1. On Melb.
cricket-ground, scene of the Austral, the bookie dare nob
bellow prices—but he can take them. Much Austral
betting goes on in Melb. streets—as much as £5OO having
been laid by an Israelite against one man in one day.
Shop-betting having been suppressed, business is now
carried on in the streets. Several men will be jigging
for over £lOOO in next Austral, and two or three for £l5OO.
• * * •
Adelaide is said to have a cycling Carbine in
the untried, 21 year-old W. A. J. Busst. He comes of the
best Australian cycle-racing family, stands sft. 10in., and
scales list. He is trained by his brother Tom, and is
Credited with some remarkable private performances.
The story goes that “he is equal to Tom at Tom’s best.”
Which means that, with 150yds. handicap, the Austral
£2OO is his.
♦ * * »
Meadham, Payne and Weaver, the Sydney
cyclists, should cut all they know for the two £lOO
Sydney prizes. Waiting for the Austral probably means
not waiting for £lOOO, nor even for one another, but for
Busst, Clinton or Matthews.
• « « «
Zim. has brought a triplet with him to do
exhibition miles, time trials, and work up generally the
glare and whoop. But only the Adelaide Oval asphalt
track, Adelaide Exhibition track, and St. Kilda
(Melb.) track, with their three laps to the mile, are suffi-
ciently dished to permit triplet pacing. On Melb. cricket-
ground, scene of the Austral, and Sydney cricket-ground,
scene of the big Sydney meeting, the flat grass tracks,
with their four laps to the mile, won’t allow a tandem
to gs its fastest. And these two are the only Melbourne
and Sydney places where Zim. can satisfactorily hip-
podrome.
• • • «
“Zim.’s” father is a New Jersey millionaire,
so that the Australian public will be able to see a cyclist
who doesn’t need to “ run stiff” in the interests of Ikey
Mo’s "bag.” Which will be a novelty after the football
season.
* * ♦ *
Neil Matterson, ex-sculler, started cycling too
late in life to set the track on fire. Athletes of his type
and age are good stayers—too good, in fact, for they are
never able to cultivate a sprint, and must, therefore, be
beaten on the post by younger and more brilliant men.
The sprint is the thing in cycling.
« « * •
Those two ,£lOO prizes at N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen’s Nov. meeting will bring out the true form of
Graeme, Wooten, Gowens, Bennett, and S. Matterson.
“Joe ” Scott is the only Melbourneite that might totally
upset the N.S.W. group, unless Morton came with
another surprise. His fourth in the 25 miles Vic. road-
race three months ago was a big performance for a
reputed non-stayer and novice.
It was Morton who paced Oathie last time, only
to be downed on the post by Payne.
» * *
Megson and Co. hope to beat Zim. from scratch.
But supposing Zim. were put on scratch with Megson for
such events as the two £lOO Sydney prizes or Melbourne
Austral’s £2OO, other competitors wouldn’t pay accept-
ance fees. They say, “ Put Zim. at least 150yds. behind
Megson in two miles."
• * • «
The fastest time the Austral (two miles) has been
won in is smin. 1 3-sseo., by Middleton last year off
186yds. mark. O’Brien has practically ridden a mile in
2.24 on Sydney cricket-ground. It would scarcely be
possible to do better than 2.18 for a mile on Melb. or
Sydney cricket-grounds, and Zim. would probably record
his two miles on either in 4.40 or 4.45.
• * ♦ «
Trainer Lee, of Melb,, has in hand Clinton and
Matthews, both as young as Busst, and as promising.
(Lee trained, among others, Bill Beach and Lambton, of
famed Austral memory.) 0. and M., on 270 and 260yds.
respectively, went down in last Austral, and having since
done nil should get good starts,
* « « «
Two of the most prominent Melb. female bikists
are dressmakers who, up to a year ago, were receiving a
pound a week each in a city house. The girls, sisters,
were not slow to see that they made the trade of the
house, so they determined to start on their own account,
doing their work at their clients’ houses. Customers
quickly bid for their services, and the pair, finding tram
locomotion too slow, began to cycle. They now do their
daily rounds on wheels, place their employes in the
houses of their customers, and, at stated intervals, ride
around to see how work progresses. They thus save rent,
and have created for themselves a status above that
enjoyed by the ordinary dressmaker.
* • * •
According to Zimmerman, the best time for a
cycled mile is Im. 57 4-sth sec.—by Johnson.
« • « •
Sydney cricket-ground on Eight Hours’ Day
had so large and varied a programme that watching
everything made the spectator as tired as the most active
competitor. The five miles amateur bicycle championship
of Australasia was contested by Reynolds (Auckland, M.L.),
Smith (Brisbane), Gillespie (Vic.), M'Donald, Barratt, and
Cameron (Sydney). The crack of the pistol sent Reynolds
right on to the hind wheel of the tandem, and the first
mile was done in 2.24 J, record time for a mile on the
Sydney track. Gillespie dropped out in the second lap,
Barratt in the third, Cameron in the fourth, and in the
fifth lap Macdonald was a hopeless second, and Smith a
hopeless third. Reynolds kept going well and merrily on
the tandem’s hind wheel, running Macdonald fairly off his
legs, and all but over-lapping him at the finish. Several of
the beaten competitors kept going on and off after first
leaving the track, and yet the committee didn’t heave a
brick at them.
The three-miles running championship saw
Blake (Vic.) and Biggs (N.S.W.) left alone after the sixth
lap (half-way). Then for the next five laps Blake kept
going vigorously, with Biggs stride for stride just behind
him. Just after the bell rang, Biggs tried to pass Blake
on the inside, and Blake, being a footballer, got
the better in this jostling, Half a lap from home the
jostling recommenced, and Biggs getting struck by
Blake’s elbow in the wind, was knocked out of the race,
and Blake won as he liked.

Published announcements of Sydney’s Saturday
cricket now take up 2J cols, in an evening paper.
* • ♦ •
The Glebe (M'Kibbin’s club) made a sensation
in Saturday and Monday’s Sydney cricket by putting up
494 for 8 wickets against Burwood. Youill’s 159 was
made without a chance in five and a half hours. M’Kibbin
scored 63 and Poidevlri 88. South Sydney beat North
Sydney, Dorman (South Sydney) being top scorer each
innings with 47 and 35 ; Syd. Gregory made 17 and 12.
Walters, the ex-Yictorian, 17 and 21, Perkins, ex-Bris-
baneite, made 0 and 7 and took 3 wickets for 21. Turner
got 1 wicket for 66 against University, while Shea, his
fellow bowler, took 7 for 37. M’Kibbin bagged 5 wickets
for 20 against Burwood, and Newell, his partner, 6 for 17.
Walford, wicket-keeping for Waverley against Paddington
(A. Bannerman’s club), stumped three men and caught
one in the second innings.
* * • •
South Sydney ought to win the local senior
cricket competition. It has nine men who have
played against a Victorian, a N.S.W. or a 6. eleven :
Gregory, Donnan, Callaway, Searle, Hume, Patrick'
Trumble, Walters (ex-Victorian), Perkins (ex-Brisbaneite).
Melb, Leg. Council is preparing an Act to ex-
tend the power of the Postmaster-General for the pur-
pose of intercepting correspondence to sweep-promoters,
&0,, in the adjoining provinces.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
The Bruin-Auraria dream-double has so in-
fatuated some Melbourneites that, unable to “get on”
down South, they sent as far as Townsville and the Towers
for this certainty, some going the length of taking the
100 to 1.
• • • •
Surprising lack of originality is shown in their
race-nomenclature by big metropolitan clubs. We have
Derhys and Newmarkets and Epsoms galore, and the
wonder is that the clubs haven’t given us a Cesarewitch
to tighten those silken threads.
• ♦ . *
Champion amateur Seale is going to box six
scientific rounds with a certain naval lieutenant. The
lieutenant, who is a stone lighter than Seale, hopped into
Sydney Amateur Athletic Club one evening and asked for
fight. Searle took him on, and often fell down before the
lieutenant s little taps upon the head. The crowd around
enjoyed the proceedings and lustily cheered the naval
hero, whom, after the spar, they bore in triumph to the
bath-room and rubbed down so vigorously that the tender
came off. So, when the lieutenant went back to
H.M.S. Pinafore, his shirt was sticking to his flesh. The
lieutenant has apologised for spoiling Seale’s reputation,
laif fi 3 aioing t 0 giVe him an °PP° rtunit y of regaining his
♦ * . «
Mr, W. R. Wilson wishes to direct the atten-
tion of all good ticket-holders to an error in The Bulle-
tin’s recent page advt. of the St. Alban’s distribution.
Five mares, Insomnia, Wild Bose, Moth, Rosette, and
Parthia, were set down as stinted, whereas they should
have come under the heading of “dry.” Mr. Wilson is
anxious to prevent any possible disappointment of lucky
speculators who may happen to win the five prizes afore-
said.
* * * *
Guineas Day, at Caulfield, has generally been a
bad time for ordinary punters. “ The Field ”is a better
horse than usual at the beginning of every Melh. racing
carnival, and the favorite can point to a long line of
historic precedents for getting upset. The rich Toorak
Handicap will probably fall to that good little mare
Gaillardia, with Common or Solanum there or there-
abouts. Also, Cook’s mare, The Parisienne, is to down
all competitors for the Guineas, and perhaps The Heroine
will cause a sensation by winning the Caulfield Stakes.
But for each of these events The Field is a good horse.
As for the Caulfield Cup, there are at least a
dozen horses that might be picked to win it, with a
special bit of good luck thrown in. Some of the most
likely ones are not certain starters thusfar. Devon, Hop-
scotch, Nobleman, Lord Richmond, and Mr. Samuel Cook
have good shows on paper.
* • • «
Trenchant has undergone a long and uninter-
rupted preparation for Caulfield Cup, and is now in such
excellent racing condition that his party are full of hope.
Piles Cured. —Owing to the fact that numbers
of people think piles are incurable, the proprietor
of the new “ Cannalin ” remedies has given in-
structions that free samples and descriptive
pamphlets are to be sent to all applicants in the
colonies. Those who have obtained no relief,
even after years of treatment or from operation,
are specially invited to write to the depdt for
Australasia, 257 George-st., Sydney, N.S.W.*
Dear Bulletin, —In your current issue
you say : —“George Black, M.P., is now a success-
ful softsgoodsman on a rather large scale in Glen
limes, N.S.W. This comes of sinking the fiscal
cptestion.” It appears to me that an insinuation
is herein contained that I, in some fashion, prob-
ably discreditable, have made enough money out
ot politics to buy a business. The allegation is as
false as it is contemptible. I have never made
one penny politically outside of my Parliamentary
allowance, and, had no other source of income
been mine, I would have been bankrupt several
times ere now. For your information, let me say
that the Parliamentary returns of four years have
hardly sufficed to cover the expenses of three elec-
tions, one “ protest,” and the thousand and one
claims to which a Labor-member, more than any
other, is subjected. My business became mine at
the cost of my mother’s death.—Yours, George
Black, N.S.W. Legislative Assembly, Oct. 3.
WOOL FREIGHTS.
The Victorian Railway Department notifies that
for Victorian-grown wools it will in future charge
for actual weight in lieu of charging at so much
per bale, as formerly. It is inconceivable why
the department held so long to an unjust and
inequitable practice. The decision of the Com-
missioners in this matter will be appreciated by
wool-growers. Doubtless, the untiring agitation
for this alteration made by the National Wool
Company, of Melbourne, will be recollected, the
company having distinctly scored in carrying the
point.*

BROKE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let’s roll our blueys up, old man.
And strike this camp V say—
“ She’s one more blooming shicei
And we gives ’er best to-day !’
I know you don’t like leavin’,
For you thought we’d make a hit 7
But cash ’n’ credit’s getting low—
So it’s nearly time to git.
There’s some patches of alluvial
In the creeks beyond Mount Claude •
Let’s try our luck ; it can’t be worse
Than this is—by the Lord !
An’ I’d like to cross the Pieman
While the tracks is fairly dry,
For they’re hell’s delight to travel
When the autumn time is by.
Yet it’s damnably disheart’nin’
When you trench ’n’ sink V drive,
And cannot square the store-bill
Ours is six pound six—or five ?
Well, it doesn’t matter which, mate.
Since we haven’t got the beans— j
But for friends which come inquirin
What this midnight marchin’ means
I will post this notice handy—
And it ought to make them smile—
“ Pardners left as stiff as crutches,
And they’re paying by the mile .
Upper Derwent, Tas.

Charleville (Q.) is about to be reticulated
with water from the bore put down in ’B9.
A lady guest (for six months) of the Gover-
nor of a Northern gaol seems to have put her foot
in it by having, on returning from the neighboring
pub., accidentally smashed the jug containing the
supper-beer of one or other of her custodians. An
enquiry is being held —in vc the supper-beer
ently—as no investigation is needed, it would seem,
in regard to prisoners beingallowed, in certain A us-
tralian “ quods,” to stroll abroad on their promising
to be “inby 9.” The writer knows aman who, from
experience, maintains that the jolliest, easiest,
and most “dolce far niente” life an Australian
can wish for is to get 12 months in some prisons
which it would not be prudent to name.
“ Crom-a-boo,” that funniest of Irish comic
football songs, is selling faster than ever.*
Years ago, in a very back-block (N.S.W.)
township, two women were charged before the
travelling P.M.—a “son of Erin’s Isle”—with
keeping a shanty. The P.M. evidently regarded
the offence as venial —at that date it was very
handy to be able to fill your flask without
reference to licensing considerations. He.
worked hard in the ladies’ favor, and eventually
dismissed the case, when up rose the prosecuting
constable and asked “his worship” what he was
to do with the jar of rum that had been seized on

Maoriland Govt, has decided to issue a
new set of stamps, after the style and size of the
Columbian issue of U.S.A. For design-prizes
(.£250) offered for competition by Govt., 2000
designs were sent from all parts, Melb. figuring
twice in the honors list (E. T. Luke, artist on the
Leader, gaining second place; Howard, of Melb.,
three seconds). Mr. Bock, a M.L. artist, gets
three first and two second prizes ; the other chief
winners are Messrs. Luke, Gaut, Voung. All
the winners have chosen Maoriland subjects.
Mr. Bock has a representation of the Roto-
mahaua Terraces, destroyed by the Tarawera
eruption, for the ss. stamp ; a view of the bounds
for the Bd. stamp, and on the Is. stamp he has
shown some kakas. Milford Sound and Mt.
Earnshaw are respectively depicted by Mr. Luke
on the 2id. and 3d. stamps. On the designs sent
in by Mr. Gaut are representations of M.L.
scenery. A picture of Mt. Cook is shown on one
of Mr. Cousins’ exhibits. The Sutherland Falls
and Mt. Egmont are the subjects chosen by Mr.
Sears. Mr. Macgregor has a view of a gold-mine
in the South Island.
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.

EXTRAORDINARY SUCCESS
m THE
TREATMENT OF OBESITY.
“ Our corpulent readers will be glad to learn how to positively lose two stone in about a month with the greatest possible benefit in health, strength, and muscle by a comparatively new system. It is a
singular paradox that the patient, returning quickly to a healthy state, with increased activity of brain, digestive and other organs, naturally requires more food than hitherto, yet, notwithstanding this, ha
absolutely loses in weight one or two pounds daily, as the weighing-machine will prove. Thus there is no suggestion of starvation. It is an absolute success, and the author, Mr. F. 0. Russell, of London,
England, who has devoted years of study to the subject, absolutely guarantees a noticeable reduction within twenty-four hours of commencing the treatment. This is different with other diseases, for the
patient in some oases may go for weeks without being able to test whether the Physician has rightly treated him, and may have derived no real or apparent improvement in health. Here, we repeat, the
author guarantees it in twenty-four hours, the scale to be the unerring judge. The treatment aims at the actual root of the disease, so that the superfluous fat does not return when discontinuing the
treatment. It is perfectly harmless. We advise our readers to call the attention of stout friends to this, because, sincerely, we think they ought to know. For their information, we may say, a book,
“Corpulency and the Cure,” 256 pages, price sixpence, containing a reprint of press notices from some hundreds of Medical and other journal's (British and Foreign), and other interesting particulars,
including the “Recipe,” can be had from Mr. F. C. Russell, Woburn House, 27 Store Street, Bedford Square, London, W.C., England, Australian Booksellers, or post free, on receipt of six penny
stamps, from GORDON & GOTCH, 123 Pitt Street, Sydney.
Mark Twain, after touring Australia and
Maoriland, will run across to Ceylon and then
visit Mauritius and South Africa. This tour will
take a year, and, at its close, he will retire to
’Murka to write a second volume of his “ Tramp
Abroad.” He was offered .£2OOO for ten lectures
in London, but that is “off ” until after the book
is finished.
N.S. W; Council, which only kicks when
its. own private interests are involved or when its
private corn is stood upon, passed Premier Reid’s
Loan Bill through committee this time with hardly
a murmur; Nobody objected much to the relief
works, and the forest-thinning job, and the re-
pairs to old dredges being passed on to posterity.
The only good thing of any importance which the
Council has done for many years past was
to block the Loan Bill last session, and, find-
ing that it had done a good thing, it got scared
there and then, and undid it with all possible
speed;
Stated on what should be unquestionable
authority that a Quinquennial Parliaments Bill
will be introduced this session in Queensland.
Triennial Parliaments was one of the original
planks of the Griffilwraith - eurn-Boodlewraith
gang ; but that’s nothing. The Nelson Ministry
will do anything to get away from its responsi-
bility to the people. To this end the agents of
the National Ass organisation, of which Nelson is
president, are using every possible device to knock
independent electors off the roll and keep them
off. To this end, once elected again, with
another subservient Parliament, they would be
glad to postpone another appeal to the country for
five years, during which bank and syndicate
schemes may breed unchecked. The devil help
Kanakaland !
W. M. Hughes, a prominent member
N.S.W. Labor-party, stated recently to a large
public meeting that the Reid Ministry didn’t want
the land or income tax, or any other democratic
measures, but was driven to support them by the
Labor members, and, in effect, that it was selling
its convictions for the sake of office. The Labor
politicians are much given to talking in this strain,
and it is woefully bad policy. If the Government
is sincere, then it is unjust as well as impolitic.
If it isn’t sincere, then it is not unjust but only
utterly foolish. Hughes and co. demand that the
Government shall do certain things, and, at the
same time, they point out carefully that if the
Government does these things it is a crawling and
wormy affair that is going down on its stomach in
the dust to save its salary. This sort of language
may at last make even G. H. Reid coalesce with
somebody or anybody in order to have the joy of
doing without Hughes and co. It is always un-
wise for the small partner to kick the large one so
long as he is doing what the small partner wants.

Judge Windeyer lias parsed many sen-
tences, but some of them would hardly bear
analysis. ’’—Orange Leader.
Oscar Wilde’s insolvency has created no
surprise in London. It was an open secret that
during tiis trial he wrote beseeching letters to his
publishers for advances of money.
Phil. Mowbray in a recent Worker :
From what I have seen of hushwomen and girls, if they
get a vote they will plump for any Syrian or Chinese
fruit-hawker that pats up. The familiarity of many girls
with niggers would be inducement to a white man suffi-
cient to seduce him, I have called the attention of
many fathers and brothers to it. There is no mincing
of the matter with me, simply because the scandal has
now become so notorious that it is often dangerous to
mention it.
The Thursday Island Japs sometimes con-
tribute to the Torres Straits Pilot. The scribe
of the community is K. Matsuoka, Esq., of the
s Japanese Club. Of certain of his enemies K.
Matsuoka caustically remarks : “ Ah ! the skin of
their foreheads is like an iron, and it being insen-
sible in any way, they are thick and hard.” A
truly pungent piece of satire, extracted from a
column of most amusing “ pidgin ” English.
The latest libel on Australian trains comes
from Bundaberg (Q.). As the tram left the
station a man was seen on the platform, waving
the departing locomotive to come back—which it
didn’t. When the train arrived at a station some
miles away, there was the missing traveller calmly
smoking and waiting on the platform. On missing
the train he had caught and saddled a horse and
ridden on in pursuit.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Eancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition;
11, a Japanese Exhibition; 12, a Wonderland;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
37, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. •
Sydney Telegraph tne other day sent its
most impressionable young reporter to interview
Mrs. Dean and Mrs. Seymour ; the result makes
rich reading. The guileless Mrs. Seymour calmly
admitted that “I did wrong 28 years ago, but I
washed my hands of it all when I came here” —
and this in face of the fact that at the original
police-court hearing and before the Dean Com-
mission she had to more or less admit having
assisted in the management of a notorious house
of ill-fame only a little more than two years ago !
Said Mrs. Dean ;
“A great many people have run away with the idea
that mother did it.”
Mrs. Seymour : “ I wasn’t there.”
Mrs. Dean: ‘‘lt lies between him and me, and 1 know
that lam innocent. I saw what Dean had said in this
morning’s Daily Telegraph. I think it is a pack of false-
hoods. I wonder he doesn’t drop."
Mrs. Seymour: 11 It is a wonder he didn’t drop.”
And when Mrs. Dean remarked that Dean was
‘‘so kind and considerate —he would have the
doctor,” Mrs. Seymour chimed in: “Of course
that was a blind—poison you first and then fetch
the doctor.” After ascertaining that Dean always
took the biggest chop for himself and gave Mrs.
Dean the little one, and left none at all for Mrs.
Seymour, the reporter left :
Just before the interview closed, the reporter asked
Mrs. Dean, who was looking exceptionally well in her
neatly-fitting navy blue dress (humph !), if she still felt
the effects of the poisons. “ Well, Idoin my feet,” she
replied ; “ when 1 stand up they pain me, and they keep
very cold. Otherwise 1 am.quite well, and have such a
good appetite, due to the strjchnine, I suppose. Mr.
Smith tells me I shall he all right when the effects totally
disappear.” “At any rate, Mrs. Dean,” the reporter
ventured to say, “your complexion does not appear to
have suffered.” “ Oh, no,” she replied, “ I always had a
good complexion ; hut, if it is better than usual, it is due
to the arsenic, I suppose.”
Tims we see how an all-wise Providence brings
good out of evil. They tried to poison Mrs. Dean
with strychnine—and it improves her appetite ;
with arsenic—and it renews her complexion.
Hallelujah !

DISINFECTION WITH "SANITAS.”
:o:
A searching investigation recently made by Dr. A. B. Griffiths, F.R.S.E., F.C.S., the well-
known Bacteriologist, has established the fact that very minute proportions of “ Sanitas”
Fluid, “Sanitas” Oil and “ Sauitas ” Emulsion suffice to quickly destroy the microbes of
Cholera, Diphtheria, Typhoid Fever, Scarlet Fever, Pneumonia, Measles, Inliuenza,
Pueiperal Fever, Glanders, Yellow Fever, &c.
It has also been shown that the vapour of “Sanitas” Oil, as generated from the
“Sanitas” Disinfecting Fumigator, has a most destructive action on the germs of
disease, and that, consequently, its inhalation must be most beneficial in the treatment
of Diphtheria, Phthisis, and all Diseases of the Lungs and Throat.
When used for fumigating sick rooms, Dr. Griffiths’ experiments show that a short time
serves to destroy all the genus that are piese it in the air.
11 is experiments also demonstrate that when “Sanitas” Fluid is sprayed about dwelling
rooms, the microbes of disease are entirely and quickly destroyed, and that small
quantities of “Sanitas” Emulsion equally well destroy the same germs when present
in water. _ Of great importance is the further fact which is brought out by Dr. Griffiths’
investigations, namely, that not only do “Sanitas” Disinfectants kill all disease germs,
but they exhibit this great advantage over oth *r preparations—namely, that they also
destroy the poisonous substances which are pro laced by disease germs and to which
they largely owe their fearful character.
Dr. Griffiths concludes his Report with these word-i;—
“There is no doubt that ‘ Sanitas ’ Oil and ‘Sanitas’ Fluid are most powerful dis-
“ infectants; consequently, they should not only be used for disinfecting rooms, hospitals,
“ barracks, prisons, &c., but also employed in the treatment of infectious diseases, such
“ as cholera, diphtheria, scarlet fever, measles, glanders, typhoid fever, tuberculosis,
“ puerperal fever, &c.”

Enid .
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For years I had longed to settle on the land. I
had developed an insatiable appetite for all that
concerned it, and read advertisements of farms to
be disposed of “ for satisfactory reasons ” much as
a connoisseur sips at a favorite old vintage. It
was a lovely dream. But, alas, it was only a
dream.
I was reporting at the time in a Maori-
land township which boasted of its two daily
papers and a population of 5000 souls.. Well, you
know what that means—writing puff-paragraphs
for every greedy little truckster in the place, and
perpetrating palpable and absurd lies about each
struggling theatrical group that managed to land
dead-broke in our midst its remnant of seedy
men who smelt of liquor, and frowsy women who
smothered a similar odour in a cloud of patchouli.
These little duties, humane enough as they may
have been, 1 did not like. Indeed, I cordially
hated them, and many a time I felt such con-
tempt for what I wrote that I could have kicked
myself round the dingy little office gleefully, and
found much relief therefrom, had the thing been
possible. It was the theatrical puffs that were
my greatest curse. Daily they had to be ground
out of my rapidly depleting brain for a whole
fortnight before the “artists” appeared, and daily
afterwards until they had managed to scrape
enough together to carry them on to the next
long-suffering hamlet, some 30 miles to the north.
On one occasion, and one only, did I try to
emancipate myself. I ventured to write, not
what I thought—that would have been simply
madness —but with a touch less of adulation than
was usual. And I had good reason for not trying
the experiment a second time. The “leading
gentleman” of the company went into the office
next morning before I arrived—mine was the
morning rag—and there was the very devil of a
din, during which every possible liberty was taken
with my name, and with my reputation as a
dramatic critic. But that did not affect me.
What did, however, was a substantial hint that
unless I could confine myself to the orthodox style
of theatrical “criticism” obtaining in that publi-
cation, my place would be filled by somebody who
could.
The period ot inflated values had just collapsed,
and the cry of the unemployed was surging nearer
home day by day. And I had a feeling that if I
lost my billet I might be driven even to the plight
of the theatrical weary-wanderer—a condition I
loathed and pitied above all others. Against that
there was the perennial hope of being able with
care to save enough to enable me to satisfy my
craving for the land. The highest flight of my
ambition was to settle down and live a life of
quiet and independence in the crisp air of the
blue hills whence sprung the babbling creek that
gave its name to the town. And so I confined
my pen to the customary rut, produced my miser-
able little paragraphs, and wrote “journalist”
after my name as a description of such work with
all the bitterness that was in me.
Things went on in much the same old way until
some six or seven months after my experiment
with the theatrical notices, when the Harris-
Delamere company appeared in our midst. They
Were a strange collection, and their arrival marked
(in epoch in the dramatic annals of that ham-
let. Never before, and never. I believe, since
have such a wardrobe and sucli stage-properties
been seen on the local boards. The glare of the
footlights may be depended on tocovera thousand
sins—l know it from experience now—bub no glare
could conceal the evidence of vicissitudes borne by
the wardrobe of the Harris-Delamere company.
But, with all their shortcomings, they had one
redeeming feature—pretty women. There were
only three of them, but all were good-looking, the
youngest of the trio particularly so. Miss Enid
Alleyn she called herself. On the stage, in
spite of the many drawbacks, she was irresistible,
and I fell a victim to her charms the first night.
In the interval the usual thing happened. The
manager strolled along, asked me how I liked the
play, and invited me to the usual nip. It was the
nipping that was one of my troubles with the
theatrical people. They seemed to look upon it
as a necessity to shout for the representatives of
the press, and it was difficult to escape from it. I
found no pleasure in the sensation that I was
drinking the money of toil-worn men and women,
who, possibly, were 'going short that my ghoulish
appetite might be appeased, or, rather, that my
contemptible’ little paragraphs might still affect
to befool a weary public. And though I made it
a practice to shout in return to satisfy what rag
of conscience was left in me, I knew all the time
what a hollow mockery it was.
That night, however, things seemed different.
I was in a spending mood, and money was
scattered freely. In tlie end I was introduced
to Miss Enid Alleyn, and . nothing would
satisfy me but to entertain her and the manager
at supper. Of this banquet I need not speak.
Suffice it to say that neither office nor lodgings
saw me again that night.
Well, of course, it was all over with me after
that. The next night, and the next, I supped
witli her again. But no manager was present on
those occasions. I was liberal with the wine as
with everything, and if my companion did not
judge her capacity so well as perhaps she might,
what was that to me? I was in love. I was
blind, and I was happy.
This sort of thing could only have one end. I
thri w in my lot with the Harris-Delamere co.,
and when they moved on to the next town I
moved with them. Farming had lost its lure.
Ambition was dead. All I desired was Enid,
and she I had.
I had saved a little money, some 70odd pounds,
for my old love, but it went by degrees into the
lap of the new. It was soon gone. How, I never
troubled to ask. But when I had no longer a
penny my new friends stuck to me bravely, and,
although I am afraid I cut but a sorry figure on
the boards, I always shared whatever there was to
share.
Thus I have existed for five years, wandering
to and fro, the shabbiest of the shabby race I
used to look upon with so much pity in the old
days. And Enid, what of her? I was deceived
and cheated. Not by her, but by myself. I
thought it was a passion of a lifetime, and I found
it but the dream of an hour. And yet I still live
on in the same old life. A hundred times I have
tried to break it off, and a hundred times I have
failed. I have not the heart or the courage to do
it. You think it very easy, no doubt. But you
are a fool. Let a habit eat into your soul and try
to shake it off by a sudden effort ; and you will
fail as I have failed.
And she ? Does she still care for me ? Ido not
know. How can I ? Somehow I contrive to
make her think I retain some spark of the old
feeling, and may she nob do the same to me ? I
might find out, bub I have nob the heart or the
courage, as I said before, to ask and to lay bare
my feelings.
And thus I live on, a human derelict.
Under the heading, “ Where shall we go
to-morrow ? ” Melb. Herald includes—funerals 1
The axe-men chain-men, &c, of the S.A.
Govt, survey parties, in addition to their princely
pay of from"4s. to 4s. 6d. per diem, receive, when
in camp, Bs. a week each as ration-allowance,
The Govt, is adding the amount of salary and
ration-allowance together, and then deducting
5 per cent, from the total in the interests of
economy. Laborers on the drainage works getting
wages at the rate of more chan .£75 a year, though
the employment may bo merely temporary, are
also penalised.
What on earth have the “law officers of
the Grown ” to do with the question of possibility
or non-possibility of debate on a parliamentary
motion? That is the Speaker’s sole business,
And a majority of the Assembly can even disagree
with Mr. Speaker’s decision, if it has determined
upon a certain course. The Premier, or the
Government, cannot forbid a debate. The “law
officers of the Crown ” have no more to do with it
than the law officers of the moon, ihe Premier,
or any other me.mber, can take a point of order
about it. The Speaker decides, and the Assembly
either submits to or disagrees with the Speaker.

with “ I pass on to the next place,” as if nothing had hap-
pened.

the tram. He said he kept a pub. this
side St. Arnaud, and that he had comedown
to Mtlb. for the show. No, he was not much
took up ” With the exhibits ; the thing that had
intertsted him most was a howling toff with

publican from this side St. Arnaud explained :
“ I thought I reckernised him, an’ after follerin’
him up a bit I was dead certain he’s the chap
what drifted into my pub. seven months ago, a
reg’lar beat, carryin’ a eighteen-penny bluey an’
lookin’ dead done up. H j slapped a letter down
on my bar, an’ arst was that good enough fer a
plain drunk. I went over it. It said he’d come
in for thirty thousan’ quid. Thinkin’ it a fake I
sent him travellin’, dry as grit, although he pro-
raised to square for all he could hold as soon as he

Henry Lawson’s book, which may be
called “Block and Backblocks : Australian Verses
by Henry Lawson,” or something of that sorb, will
be out shortly and will, like “ Banjo’s ” (orders
for which are rapidly flowing in), be a handsome
volume printed in new type on fine paper. The
price will be ss. Gd., post free from this office, and
The Bulletin, after seeing the material of which
the book is composed, has no hesitation in saying
that it deserves to be, and will be, an immediate
and permanent success.
A woman was found dead, the other night,
in a Sydney lodging-house, lying on the floor,
devoid of any comforts, and absolutely starved.
Yet she had £7O in cash upon her ! What sane
mind can fathom, can even affect the faintest
perception of, the mania of the miser? Query :
Does the miser feel the pangs of hunger ?_ Does
the miser feel cold ? Have the mental scientists
ever discovered answers to these questions? Is a
miser ansestheticised by his mania ?
According to authoritative statements at a
Melb. municipal conference, 5’6 per cent, of all
cattle locally slaughtered are afflicted with tuber-
culosis. Cattle fatten more quickly after being
seized with the germs of the disease. Marasmus
in children is due to tubercle in the bowel, usually
contracted from the milk imbibed. Melb. city
abattoirs are the only ones under effective super-
vision, and they deal with but a fraction of the
meat-supply, because slaughtermen prefer the lax
supervision, or no-supervision, of other abattoirs.
There is no reason why the municipalities should
nob be given an absolute control of the meat-
supply.
“ Canada,” writes an expatriated Austra-
lian to a Bulletin correspondent, “ is no goodlor
the laborer. It is a country of vast monopolies.
Sioux Indians, Chinese, Japanese, are the laborers
of Canada. I believe the Bulletin is right—
Australia is the best country in the whole world
for the workman. Labor’s pay is ss. per day of
10 hours on the Pacific Coast, and on the Atlantic
Coast only 3s. 9d. per day of 10 hours ; the
general pay in the East of Canada has not been
any better for the last 10 years, and very little
pay or work can be got at that price. After pay-
ing for board your wages at the best are 2s. 6d.
per day for 10 hours’ work, and you are lucky to
get employment even at that price.”
Dear Bulletin, —N.S. W. Lands Minister
Carruthers proposes to abolish the ballot between
rival claimants for selection, and make the Land
Boards arbiters. The ballot has its disadvantages;
bub the other is a doubtful expedient, even if the
House would sanction it. It plays into the hands of
theland-agents and places the Boards in an awkward
position. Also, the Land Boards would have to
sib much more frequently than they now do.
Fancy half-a-dozen applicants for a certain block,
each with as good a case as the others. They pay
their deposits and wait three months for the Board
to sib. The certain result is five would-be selectors
with a rankling grievance against the Law and the
administration. Kissing goes by favor ! Better
stick to the ballot. — Treble B.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Miss Riley, let me introjuce Mr. Hough-
fieigh—Miss Riley, Mick and he stood
back with an ambiguous smile that nettled Miss
Riley as she extended her No. 11 hand. Mr.
Houghfleigh was delighted, ho was sure. (Ho
had studied etiquette books.) Miss Riley was
Irish, and had a full, fetching figure, and an
attractive face —for Mick. Mick was anything.
He had been a Dane for a week when Christina
Petersen was en suite.
Said Miss Riley to Mick, soon after their ac-
quaintance —“ Hwhat’s your name ? Oi didn’t get
it ’krict, Oi think.”
“Houghfleigh—Mick; no, Moichael Hough-
fleigh ’ —with a little irritation, and a good deal
of determination.

Mick’s “ould man” had come down for
the splicing, and whisky. Messieurs and
madame Houghfleigh adjourned to the vestry for
caligraphic purposes. The school had lived a long

He took up the pen rather shakily. Madame was

“ Jhust shtand a whon side Bridgie, till Oi git
some loight.”

TOM GAINDAR.
The most ingenious contrivance of modern
thief history is an affair like a wire sponge
basket, which, inserted in a pillar-box, is invisible,
but which catches every letter posted by the
unwary public.
Dear Bulletin, —You sometimes gibe at
Hingland’s custom of taking full credit for the
work of Scotchmen or Irishmen. Why not look
nearer home for a joke? Peter Jackson, the black-
fellow, is a genuine “ Australian champion ”
according to A.N.A. notions. A violinist, named
Johann Kruse, has been paying a flying visit from
Europe to his presumably native Victoria, and
the Melb. dailies “blow” about him with much
vigor. Thus the Argus : “Mr. Kruse’s reading
of the concerto was that of a cultured, musicianly
mind. . . . At the conclusion the audience
recognised in the artist who had presented this
superb panorama before its mental vision a young
Australian whom it delighted to honor.” The
Age concedes a point to the artist’s very German
name by generally calling him Herr, but thinks
that “regarded as a Vic. product Johann Kruse
is a violinist of whom we all have a right to feel
nroud. It is, in a sense, true, of course, that we
only supplied the raw material, and that the
credit of working it up belongs to Germany.”
The raw material, observe, was of German origin
and has been worked up in the land it sprang
from, yet Herr Kruse, whose family can’t have
been settled in Victoria for much more than fifty
years, anyway, is a Victorian of whom we ought
to feel proud.—Yours, Max Fieck.
“ Man should be made to assist in the
domestic work —even to wash the dishes,” says
the secretary of the Melb. Woman’s Franchise
League. The dividing of the ways :
Dear Bulletin, —In reply to “ P. Luffcig s
query, let me say that there are no alligators in
Queensland. The southernmost station of the
crocodile is the Boyne River in the Port Curtis
district, where I saw several large specimens about
ten years ago. What restricts the species so
narrowly to the tropic of Capricorn ? Not tem-
perature, for I have seen it in the cold mountain
streams in the interior of New Guinea. Probably
the beast’s coasting certificate allowed him to
travel from river-mouth to river-mouth south-
wards within the shelter of the Barrier reef, but
not to navigate the rough open sea round Bustard
Head. Beyond Australia this species ranges east
to the Solomons, north to the Philippines, and
west to Ceylon. And now allow me to ask a query
in turn. What is the northern limit of the
platypus? My furthest record is Baffle Greek,
i.e., platypus ends where crocodile begins. Has
the crocodile exterminated the little chap?—
Charles Hedlkv. Australian Museum, Sydney.
Sept. 27, 1895.

The Sultan of Morocco, at whose secret
signal the other day his troops suddenly
massacred the leaders of some troublesome tribes-
men who were standing among the spectators at a
military review, is a boy of 15 years. A most
promising youth.
“Snell” : The Bulletin, by spreading
the idea that crude kerosene is an excellent
fertiliser for the hair, brought about a serious
accident in a Melbourne barber’s shop, the other
night. lam informed by the presiding artist, a
worthy young man who ’tends church and whose
tote is conducted on moral principles, that a
regular customer, a musician, turned up as usual
to be burnt off, but as soon as the lighted taper
touched his hair, which was very profuse, the mass
ignited, and the musician jumped from his chair
witli a fearful yell and charged out of the shop.
When the barber pulled himself together and
followed his flaming customer, he found the pro-
fessor standing on his head in a horse-trough,
with the kindly assistance of a constable, who
wanted to know if the man was insured, and
seemed disposed to arrest the “clip” for arson.
Petroleum may be a good top-dressi a g, but it has
its drawbacks.
A Maoriland paper : “ Benjamin Shoure,
who is said to be an advocate of the single-tax,
took a fatal dose of poison.” Shoure was an
ardent single-taxer, and when he arrived at
Wellington from Europe he was so crushed by the
public indifference about George’s great theory,
that he took a large dose of morphia. Ho was
baled out at the hospital and recovered. Then he
removed to Sydney, and was so oppressed by the
want of interest in the single-tax there that he
took more morphia. A policeman found him in-
sensible on a wharf, and rushed him off to the
hospital, and he was resuscitated a second time.
Then he went to Auckland, and after a vain
endeavour to convert the people of that city to the
single-tax theory, he evidently decided that a
world which wasn’t in a state of frantic excite-
ment about the single-tax wasn’t worth living in,
so he swallowed a large quantity of morphia and
died. What a lot of single-taxers have wild,
morphia-looking eyes ! The Bulletin can count
up at least a dozen in Sydney alone who carry
either the stamp of morphia or the bulgy glare of
epilepsy. It is a ghastly thing to be dreadfully
in earnest.
An American firm has just re-printed
“ The Secret Memoirs of the Court of Louis XIV.
and the Regency,” which consist of a compilation
of extracts from the correspondence of his august
relative, the Duchess D’Orleans. They are spicy
yarns. Here is a sample : —“ Henri IV., hearing
that one of his mistresses was unfaithful, learned
that she had an assignation on a particular even-
ing at her own apartments with the Due do Belle-
ville. Just as supper-hour arrived, Henri knocked
and found the table prepared for two. There
were a couple of partridges and a loaf of bread on
the cloth, and, after a few commonplaces, Henri
drew up chairs for himself and his hostess. All
proceeded swimmingly, when suddenly the royal
visitor out off half the loaf, scooped out the
crumb, jammed one of the partridges into the
cavity, and fired it under the bed. ‘ Sire !’
exclaimed the terrified lady, ‘ what are you
doing ?’ 4 Madame,’ returned the merry monarch
as he rose, 'everybody must live.’ Making ( a
graceful adieu, he forthwith quitted the scene.”

The Burial Service of a Musician .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We gathered around the open grave, ten of us, and
gazed down silently at the plain deal coffin. None
of us knew any burial-service, but something of
the sort seemed necessary, so we stood puzzling
our brains to recall some good action of the
deceased. After a lapse of several minutes Jones
mentioned how the corpse had once treated
Cornish Joe to a big burst. The remark was
better loft unsaid, for we all remembered that
the sequel to that action was his running through
the traveller’s pockets and abstracting all the con-
tents thereof.
There was another silence of five minutes while
we moved our feet about, shuffling the loose earth
into rude circles. Then Darbyshire whispered
that perhaps if we sang something it would be
better than keeping dead silence. There was no
answer to Darbyshire’s suggestion.
By and bye, Murphy could stand it no longer.
“ Boys,” he cried, “ d’ye moind the toime he did
Ginger Smith outer a pen at Gerogery ? A low,
sneaking hound he was.”
“Aye, he was that,” chipped in M'Dougall.
“Do you no remember him saltin’ the South
claim, too ? ”
We all remembered it well, Our tongues were
loosened at once, and each of us had some anec-
dote to relate of the perfidy of the departed. For
an hour or more we stayed, until the pauses got
longer between the yarns, and ultimately conver-
sation came to a stop. We shovelled in the earth
and left a yellow mound to mark the spot. Then
we made for the Miner’s Arms to drink the corpse’s
health.
“Fill ’em up, mon,” said M'Dougall. “He
was a dom’d scoondrel, but, my oath, he could
play the Jew’s harp !”
BONDINGE.
What a Colling wood (Melb.) woman dosed
her husband with, the other night: Castor-oil and
milk, brandy, linseed poultice, a go of salts, a cup
of tea, and three-penn’orth of rum (hot). And
yet it was five hours before that iron-framed
man died in a fit. But the poor soul meant
well. Where is the man whose wife is not angry
if he does not eat ?
Victoria is positively struggling along
without any assistance from the delectable Davies
family, a state of things undreamt-of in the by-
gone boom-time. The other day an opportunity
offered for “chucking” J. M. Davies out of the
Leg. Council, and he had to go. J.M. was the
pious, prudent Davies who ever maintained a
beatific front. Fat Man is sorry to lose him
from the Council. Lawyer Godfrey was the
valuable means of unseating J.M. Among the
comparatively Radical property-owners of South
Melbourne there was a strong majority for God-
frey, on the ground that he used to kick up
a row at depositors’ meetings. Twenty per
cent, of all the votes polled in this fight came
from South Melbourne, and so the retiring mem-
ber was retired for an indefinite period.
